>>I know good programming when I see it.

Oh shucks, you're just easily impressed.

>>>In other News... trains run on time, garbage gets collected, cops arrest 
>>>the right criminal with no trouble at all.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

If you'll reread The Road To Wigan Pier, you'll find Orwell was a fan of socialism. He was
describing British middle class contempt for the working class, and the working class's
contempt for socialists, whom they saw as leftist nonconformists. 

Like Orwell, you're confusing taste with vanity and wrapping both in a bundle labeled
snobbery. The difference is this -- the vain snob has a perception of quality in his own
mind; the craftsman elicits a perception of quality in ANOTHER's mind.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol coders: Going, going, gone?

_(thread depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-02T21:35:40+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6pkaatF8fha9U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<cd4fb054-9473-4ecb-a57c-2e57fa91788d@l42g2000yqe.googlegroups.com> <bd41j45l60nj7t5u4jgov0u3540drpr5c2@4ax.com> <ggrate$2p6$1@reader1.panix.com> <p9c4j492t5tr978af17853ihbp7ag91566@4ax.com> <gh0u6p$1hd$1@reader1.panix.com> <4vd9j4l3ktc187qn0nnosg1gq5o66p9amu@4ax.com>`

```
Robert wrote:
> On Mon, 1 Dec 2008 15:00:42 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> A few in books and magazines. Early McCracken was good.

I have to disagree here, Robert.

McCracken was the "standard" COBOL text for many COBOL courses in Polytechs 
during the 60s and 70s.

I thought it was apalling and I wouldn't use it for teaching purposes.

On the other hand, "COBOL Logic and Programming" by Dr. Fritz D. MacCameron 
of Louisiana State University was light years ahead of McCracken. He gives 
the best explanation of nested IFs and complex PERFORMS that I have ever 
seen before or since, and I still use his approach to explain these things 
to new COBOL programmers when I am required to.

Perhaps beauty is indeed in the eye of the beholder... :-)

Pete.
```

will be aware of or knowledgeable about only a small fraction of what
the government is doing, the major ability has to be getting other
good people and then working with and through them to run the
government.  Rightly or wrongly he had the ability to get support for
much of what he believed to be needed.  Somehow I suspect much of the
dislike for both G W Bush and Clinton came from there abilities to get
things even though they were perceived as being lacking by their
opponents.

For those who like complexity, take a look at the financial
instruments that have been in the news.  The simpler a solution, the
more likely it is to succeed purely because it is understood.  There
are dangers to both simplicity and complexity.

Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Sun, 23 Nov 2008 18:20:30 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Actor's Guild.

While I'm not a Reagan fan, I have to say that Pete is grossly
underrating him. Archival work has shown that he was a serious policy
wonk, at least during his days as governor; we have extensive
documentation demonstrating his in-depth research and sophisticated
understanding of complex policy topics.

And while Reagan was best known for B-list work (and won the first
Razzy for "Worst Career"), he was in a number of features that won or
were nominated for significant awards, such as "The Killers", "King's
Road", and the (in)famous "Knute Rockne All American".

For that matter, his early work as a radio baseball announcer shows
how quickly he thought on his feet, at least as a young man. He sat in
a room reading a ticker-tape printout of what was happening in the
game, and based on that would narrate the game in real time - making
up all the details so it would sound as if he were actually there.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6qj8idFcm58gU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> A "B-movie" actor is not a "celebrity."

There is a cult following for Reagan movies... that has nothing to do with 
clebrity? Gosh, I guess it must be the intrinsic value of the films... dunno 
how I missed it, I'm usually very positive... : - )
>
> Reagan got our hostages released from Iran, destroyed the Soviet
> Union, re-united Germany, and, let's not forget, invaded Grenada.

Ross Perot (and others) got THEIR hostages out of Iran long before the US 
government was able to achieve it. Hostage release involved a large amount 
of behind-the-scenes negotiation by a number of countries. However, I would 
not deny that Reagan was a  major contributor.

The Soviet Union destroyed itself.  It simply couldn't go on... The Internet 
may have been a contributing factor; when kids start talking to each other 
they form pictures of how other kids live. They then grow up and decide to 
do something about it. I would give Gorbachov far more credit for the reform 
of Soviet Russia than I would Reagan, although the US (under Reagan) was a 
significant contributor and backer for the revolutions in Poland and Hungary 
which started the ball rolling in 1989.

The re-unification of Germany was a topic dear to the hearts of most Germans 
in the East and West. Helmut Kohl was determined to see it happen and, 
riding a wave of misplaced emotion, he pushed through a deal which was very 
disadvantageous to his own people. In effect, he was out-negotiated by the 
East and miscalculated the wealth and resilience of West Germany. The 
recovery is still going on 18 years later (some pundits estimate it will be 
a 25 year exercise to get back to where they were in 1990). By 1994 the 
German people realised Kohl had made a mistake, as the prosperity he 
promised failed to materialise. Although he was the first Chancellor since 
Adenauer to resume connections with the US, and he did communicate with 
Reagan, he also did the same with his East German counterpart. I don't see 
German unification as a victory for Reagan; more an expensive mistake for 
West Germany. Certainly, it was desirable and would have been inevitable in 
the long run, but Kohl got the terms badly wrong.

I make no comment on the invasion of Grenada... : - )

Pete.
```

On Nov 12, 8:58 am, mario <mmc_vw1...@hotmail.com> wrote:
> hi

Depending on what company you work for, you probably already have
software that does this, so there's no need to reinvent the wheel.  I
work for Infogix (formerly known as Unitech Systems), which makes
software that does duplicate file checking, and does the hashing
you're talking about using a CRC32 routine.  Most large shops have our
software, so ask around, you might find this task will take you only a
few minutes if you find the software.  Look for ACR/Summary or Infogix
Assure.

Vivian
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: string to correct integer

```
Karl Kiesel wrote:
> "mario" <mmc_vw1200@hotmail.com> schrieb im Newsbeitrag
> news:fdaf12f9-a2bc-4caf-b0b2-e7a066be81f7@g38g2000yqn.googlegroups.com...
> hello.
>
<snip>

>
> Looking up 'intrinsic functions' in your COBOL manual, may be you find
> soemthing usable there??

Always good advice :-)

NUMVAL, NUMVAL-C,  and TEST-NUMVAL are good, but they are limited.

Specifically, they don't provide a choice of numeric formats, assuming that 
the target is the only format you want, they don't validate but expect a 
valid numeric string,  and they don't tell you how many digits each side of 
the point.

I believe TEST-NUMVAL is part of the 2002 standard and, as such, has never 
been implemented as far as I know....

I use a component called String2Num for all of these reasons.

The component can not only validate, but it can notify the User as well, if 
required, and give examples of correct usage. It can also run on a web page 
or the desktop. (I don't think function NUMVAL can be invoked from 
JavaScript, but you could probably use it if you were writing .NetCOBOL for 
.NET and using it to script a web page).

Pete.
```

The 'string' must be null terminated.
To use C library functions the compiler must be capable of doing so,
not all are, and the library may need to be specified in the link.

While the OP said 'integer' he expanded this to requiring 'decimal
places'.

```
On Dec 2, 10:36 pm, mario <mmc_vw1...@hotmail.com> wrote:
> hello.
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> places (1234,5678)

If it has decimal places then it is not an 'integer'.


> for example i get a string  1234,123   and call my perfect programm
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> and i´m sure i´m not the only one with that problem ;)

You can use UNSTRING to split the string into parts delimited by '.'
or space. Each of the resulting parts can be INSPECTed to get their
length and then each can be tested using reference notation with an IF
NUMERIC.

     given a non-space alleged numeric in csv_string
     code may be approximately:

     move all zero   to resulting-parts
     move 1          to resulting-negative
     move 1          to csv-pointer
     inspect csv-string
         tallying csv-pointer for leading spaces

     move zero      to part-subscript
     perform
        with test after
        until part-length = zero
          and part-end = space

        unstring csv-string
            delimited by "." or "-" or "+" or space
            into part-string
                 delimiter in part-end
                 count in part-length
            with pointer csv-pointer

       if ( part-length not = zero )
          if ( part-string(1:part-length) not numeric )
              error - not numeric
           else
              move function NUMVAL(part-string(1:part-length))
                 to resulting-number(part-subscript)
          end-if
       end-if

       if ( part-end = "." )
           add 1   to part-subscript
           if ( part-subscript > 2 )
               error - too many .
           end-if
       end-if
       if ( part-end = "-" )
           move -1  to resulting-negative
       end-if
       ....


You should add checks for part-length exceeding the limits that you
require.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: string to correct integer

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>

```
Back before I had functions to do such conversions, this kind of code
wasn't infrequent.    But it was simple enough that I never thought to
create a copy member or a called program to do it.

This was before objects got popular, and integrating too many programs
simply meant more difficult testing, harder to follow programs, and
more maintenance concerns.   Isolation was valued.

OO has resulted in tools that lessen those issues, but still this is
simple code for a batch CoBOL environment such as the one where I
work.
```

#### ↳ Re: string to correct integer

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-12-02T22:20:52-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<v9ubj4l6jdg5thb635md29dqqva4r4l0qp@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<fdaf12f9-a2bc-4caf-b0b2-e7a066be81f7@g38g2000yqn.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Tue, 2 Dec 2008 01:36:59 -0800 (PST), mario <mmc_vw1200@hotmail.com> wrote:

>hello.
>
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>and iï¿½m sure iï¿½m not the only one with that problem ;)

       @OPTIONS SRF(FREE,FREE)
   identification division   
 . program-id. aton-driver
 . author. Robert Wagner

* Parses & normalizes a number, checking for errors
* Test driver on top, function below

 . data division
 . working-storage section
 . 01  input-area                pic  x(30)
 . 01  output-number             pic s9(09)v9(09) 

 . procedure division.
     perform read-input
     perform  until input-area = spaces
         call 'aton' using input-area, output-number
         if  return-code not equal to zero
             display 'error ' return-code
         else
             display output-number
         end-if
         perform read-input
     end-perform
     goback
 . read-input.
     accept input-area from console

 . identification division
 . program-id. aton common
*> Parses & normalizes a number, checking for errors
*> Errors: 1=overflow, 2=underflow, 3=multiple periods, 4=multiple minus, 5=invalid
character
 . data division
 . working-storage section
 . 01  unqualified-variables
 .     05  n                     binary   pic s9(04)
 .     05  lr                    binary   pic s9(04)
 .     05  filler1                        pic  x
 .         88  minus-seen                 value 'y' 

 . linkage section
 . 01  input-area 
 .     05  input-byte occurs 30 
 .         10  input-byte-n      pic 9
 . 01  output-number             pic s9(09)v9(09)
 . 01   redefines output-number
 .     05  output-left           pic  9(09)
 .     05  output-right
 .         10  output-byte occurs 9  pic x

 . procedure division using input-area, output-number.
       initialize unqualified-variables, output-number
       perform varying n from 1 by 1 until n > length of input-area
           evaluate input-byte (n)
               when space
                   continue
               when '0' thru '9'
                   if  lr equal to zero
                       multiply 10 by output-left 
                           on size error move 1 to return-code goback end-multiply
                       add input-byte-n (n) to output-left
                   else
                       if  lr greater than length of output-right
                           move 2 to return-code goback
                       end-if
                       move input-byte (n) to output-byte (lr)
                       add 1 to lr
                   end-if
               when '.'
                   if  lr equal to zero
                       move 1 to lr
                   else
                       move 3 to return-code goback
                   end-if
               when '-'
                   if  not minus-seen
                       set minus-seen to true
                   else
                       move 4 to return-code goback
                   end-if
               when ','
               when "'"
               when '"'
               when x'00'
               when '/' 
               when '+'
                   continue
               when other
                   move 5 to return-code goback   
           end-evaluate
       end-perform
       if  minus-seen
           compute output-number = 0 - output-number
       end-if
       move zero to return-code goback
 . end program aton
 . end program aton-driver
 .

Here's a challenge. If the CSV is created by Excel, it might contain exponential notation
like 1.23E+2. Make the function recursive so it can call itself to normalize the exponent,
then apply it to the output with **. I tried, Fujitsu choked on recursion, so I deleted
it.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: string to correct integer

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-07T11:19:14+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6q0c34Fa97afU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<fdaf12f9-a2bc-4caf-b0b2-e7a066be81f7@g38g2000yqn.googlegroups.com> <v9ubj4l6jdg5thb635md29dqqva4r4l0qp@4ax.com>`

```
Robert wrote:
> On Tue, 2 Dec 2008 01:36:59 -0800 (PST), mario
> <mmc_vw1200@hotmail.com> wrote:
…[129 more quoted lines elided]…
> output with **. I tried, Fujitsu choked on recursion, so I deleted it.

Robert,

a few observations...

1. The code above (in my opinion...) is not a proper solution for a true 
numeric validation of screen input. It doesn't handle a number of 
conditions. (multiple or conflicting signs, embedded blanks,  CR/DR leading 
or trailing, with or without a blank, presence of a currency symbol) and it 
is not suitable for European formats.

2. It doesn't provide alternate numeric formats for output (this is not 
"wrong". but it is very useful to have)

3. It doesn't tell us how many digits are on each side of the point. (A 
requirement specified by the OP).

4. I have accepted your challenge and extended String2Num to handle 
scientific notation. It does not require recursion to move a decimal point 
and pad with zeros right or left. (However, I do expect the string tohave 
been normalized and to be in base 10; if it is coming from EXCEL, this will 
be the case, just as in your example above. I also expect the floating point 
format to contain a point.(It can be a comma or a period), and the string 
MAY be explicitly signed with a leading or trailing sign and/or CR/DR. There 
are a number of different floating point formats, as I'm sure you are 
aware.) I would normally have written this as a separate method, but I broke 
my own "rule" and added a section to the base code, because I expected it to 
be trivial... : - ) In the event, it was not... Nevertheless, it was quite 
relaxing and enjoyable to write COBOL again after a long absence. It still 
took about 3 times as much code as it would have in C#, mainly because I 
couldn't use Regular Expressions to pick things up, and had to use INSPECT 
instead.

(I got a surprise when I found that using the REVERSE function with INSPECT 
precludes replacement; Fujitsu grumbled that function REVERSE cannot be used 
as a target. This meant one INSPECT using REVERSE, then another to do the 
biz. A single REGEX would do the lot.)

5. Fujitsu does not "choke on recursion" if you use the OO COBOL facilities. 
(ALL COBOL is non-recursive according to the standard, so it is hardly fair 
to single out Fujitsu...) OO COBOL handles recursion through the use of 
object storage and the ability to instantiate new instances of an object 
from the object itself.

Pete.
```

The '02 Standard includes support for "recursion" (as well as the related 
"local-storage section").  A number of vendors have already implemented this 
feature - even when they haven't implemented the full '02 Standard.  I know that 
both IBM and Micro Focus have, there are probably others.

See (for example)
 http://publibfp.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr/BOOKS/IGY3LR40/3.1

and following for the "IS RECURSIVE" phrase of the Program-ID paragraph.

P.S.  This also relates to a comment I made a while ago about Fujitsu NOT 
staying "current" with IBM mainframe COBOL dialects - for those interested in 
"porting" IBM mainframe COBOL applications to the workstation environment.

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-07T15:29:08-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<FeX_k.320609$4p1.318080@en-nntp-09.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<fdaf12f9-a2bc-4caf-b0b2-e7a066be81f7@g38g2000yqn.googlegroups.com> <v9ubj4l6jdg5thb635md29dqqva4r4l0qp@4ax.com> <6q0c34Fa97afU1@mid.individual.net> <BtD_k.409654$C65.11138@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com> <6q15nlFa9t85U1@mid.individual.net> <YCK_k.383146$vK2.16250@en-nntp-03.dc1.easynews.com> <6q2gtoFaf8opU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
The most commonly used MAJOR extension in IBM's recent COBOL compilers is its 
"native" XML support.  It would seem to me that SOME of the sites that might 
want to look at a Fujitsu conversion may be the sites that have used this 
feature.

The feature of "IS RECURSIVE" which started this discussion, has been used by 
some customers, but is certainly not universal.  Local-Storage, on the other 
hand, is relatively common (as it gets "storage" from a different place under LE 
than Working-Storage does).  I think that Fujitsu does support this in non-OO 
programs, so it shouldn't be a problem.

The other issue, of course, is that more and more IBM mainframe COBOL programs 
are using LE callable services.  I know that Micro Focus had "emulators" for 
these, but I don't think that Fujitsu does.  This wouldn't be a problem for 
converting "source code" but would be for converting applications.

  ***

As for why I think that Fujitsu has NOT be staying current (besides lack of "IS 
RECURSIVE" support and EXEC XML support), up until this year, I stayed current 
on my Fujitsu maintenance and was very careful in reading their LRM and "What's 
new" documentation and saw that they did NOT include any "new" IBM dialect 
features in any of their releases.  Many of the "post-LE" IBM extensions were 
"relatively common" extensions (e.g. PROCEDURE-POINTER) and I think that Fujitsu 
may have those already (for their own use).  However, when IBM made changes 
(such as SET ADDRESS for working-storage items, changed size limits for GLOBs 
and BLOBs for SQL, etc) I never saw that Fujitsu kept current (while MF did).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Fujitsu and IBM mainframe compatibility (was: Recursion in Standard (was: string to correct integer

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-07T20:30:24-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6F%_k.411558$C65.360459@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<fdaf12f9-a2bc-4caf-b0b2-e7a066be81f7@g38g2000yqn.googlegroups.com> <v9ubj4l6jdg5thb635md29dqqva4r4l0qp@4ax.com> <6q0c34Fa97afU1@mid.individual.net> <BtD_k.409654$C65.11138@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com> <6q15nlFa9t85U1@mid.individual.net> <YCK_k.383146$vK2.16250@en-nntp-03.dc1.easynews.com> <6q2gtoFaf8opU1@mid.individual.net> <FeX_k.320609$4p1.318080@en-nntp-09.dc1.easynews.com> <6q391hFaeoqbU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
General reply (especially to the "WHY" question)

I could be totally mistaken on this, but it is (sort-of?) my impression that 
Fujitsu has handed the "ongoing IBM mainframe development" market to Micro Focus 
(and to a lesser extent IBM itself.  MF provides all the "new" bells & whistles 
ever time there is a new release of the COBOL compiler (or CICS or DB2 or IMS). 
This means that MF customers can continue using their products for doing "new 
and interesting" development/maintenance on the Workstation but targeted for 
mainframe production.  OBVIOUSLY, MF also provides a whole lot of solutions for 
those porting production to other platforms as WELL, but it is this development 
market that Fujitsu doesn't seem to be keeping current with.  (Of course, with 
the "replacement" Fujitsu company, I don't know how this will go in the future.)

Those LARGE IBM shops that are keeping LARGE applications running (and being 
developed/maintained) on the mainframe, can also afford "package deals" for the 
MF pricing policies.

Fujitsu seems (to me) to be targeting those IBM mainframe shops that are 
migrating their COBOL production off the mainframe.  Most (certainly not all) of 
those shops are ones that haven't been current on IBM COBOL compiler maintenance 
and certainly have NOT been using all the new features that IBM has offered in 
recent compilers.

There is also the historical issue going back to the ANCIENT IBM/Fujitsu law 
suites and the fact that Fujitsu had access to the original IBM VS COBOL II 
compiler source code.  This meant that they provided EXCELLENT compatibility 
with that IBM compiler.  However, once LE and the related IBM COBOL compilers 
came along, Fujitsu no longer had access to the compiler (or run-time) source 
code.  Ever since then, they have done minimal (if any) enhancements for IBM 
mainframe compatibility.
```

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>

I wrote an application that parsed and normalized printed reports, which were usually
formatted by Excel. It DID encounter scientific notation .. not very often, but enough to
warrant programming for it. 

The requirement to de-edit numbers is common in financial data warehousing, because laws
requiring publication of reports are still oriented to paper format. Millions of examples
can be found at http://www.sec.gov/edgar.shtml, which was our primary source for US
companies. Canadian was worse, because they were published in .pdf format. It's easy to
put stuff INTO .pdf; Adobe deliberately makes it difficult to get anything OUT.

A posting being reversed in the following month, called an accrual entry, will have a
second record dated next month with the sign reversed (or debit and credit accounts
exchanged). I can see where that might create a double negative. The parser should return
a positive when it sees a double negative.

What we need is a TRIM function and a VARCHAR data type.

01  varchar typedef.
     05  string-length  binary pic s9(04).
     05  string.
           10  string-byte occurs 0 to 30 depending on string-length.

function-id. trim.
linkage section.
01  cobol-string    pic x(30).
01  varchar-string varchar.
procedure division using cobol-string returning varchar-string.
    move function stored-char-length (cobol-string) to string-length
    move cobol-string to string
   goback.

reasons described in the "Components 101" white paper.)

There are disagreements about the RegEx standards and different dialects of 
RegEx syntax (just like most things). Perl is not as favoured as it was, but 
as COBOL has NO innate RegEx support, this library could be a useful 
addition.


>>> If I were doing this for real, I wouldn't step through 30 bytes of
>>> input spaces. I'd set input length by testing the rightmost 21 for
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>
(I'm not sure that "occurs 0 ..." is OK in COBOL, but I agree that if it 
isn't, it should be... : - ))

C# provides both "Trim" and "TrimRight" functions as innate methods of the 
base "String" class. "Trim" trims both left and right.

Your interesting little snippet above doesn't trim leading spaces, but would 
work fine as a "TrimRight" function. I'm not sure that it is adding much 
value to the STORED-CHAR-LENGTH  function (and it would only work with 
Fujitsu), but it is an imaginative approach and the idea of extending COBOL 
with user written functions is a good one. (Of course, you realise that this 
violates the "rule" that a function cannot change data... see discussions 
elsewhere : - ))

Based on your idea, here's my extension of it...

 01  varchar typedef.
     05  string-length  binary pic s9(04).
     05  string.
           10  string-byte occurs 1 to 255 depending on string-length.

 function-id. TrimRight.
 working-storage section.
 01  ctr pic s9(4) comp.
 linkage section.
 01  cobol-string    pic x(255).
 01  varchar-string varchar.
 procedure division using cobol-string returning varchar-string.
    move zero to ctr
    inspect function REVERSE (cobol-string)
        tallying ctr
        for leading spaces
    compute ctr = 256 - ctr
    move ctr to string-length
    move cobol-string (1: ctr) to string
    goback
    .

 function-id. Trim.
 linkage section.
 01  cobol-string    pic x(255).
 01  varchar-string varchar.
 procedure division using cobol-string returning varchar-string.
   perform until cobol-string (1:1) NOT = space
        move cobol string (2: 254) to cobol-string
   end-perform
   move function Trim (cobol-string) to varchar-string
   goback
   .

Obviously, you could use indexing instead of left shifting in the second 
example. It comes down to whether there are likely to be many leading spaces 
and your personal preference.

I believe that both these functions should work on any COBOL platform (that 
supports Typedef...), but I haven't compiled/debugged them.


>>> Bill Klein answered this well. One can get around it by programming
>>> the stack as an array and using PERFORM rather than CALL. I did it
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> stop referring to '85 as Cobol II.

And  around 20 years to get a Standard after 85...

Pete.
```

```
On Tue, 9 Dec 2008 13:28:38 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>Robert wrote:

>> What we need is a TRIM function and a VARCHAR data type.
>>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>base "String" class. "Trim" trims both left and right.

Can't one use C# methods in OO Cobol (compiled to CLR)? Problem solved. 

>Your interesting little snippet above doesn't trim leading spaces, but would 
>work fine as a "TrimRight" function. I'm not sure that it is adding much 
…[44 more quoted lines elided]…
>and your personal preference.

Your code is asymmetrical. I'd use INSPECT to trim both left and right.

 function-id. Trim.
 working-storage section.
 01  left-spaces pic s9(4) binary.
 01  right-spaces pic s9(04) binary.
 linkage section.
 01  cobol-string    pic x(255).
 01  varchar-string varchar.
 procedure division using cobol-string returning varchar-string.
   initialize left-spaces, right-spaces
   inspect cobol-string
        tallying left-spaces
        for leading spaces
   inspect function reverse (cobol-string)
        tallying right-spaces
        for leading spaces
   compute string-length = length of cobol-string - left-spaces - right-spaces
   if  string-length less than zero
       move zero to string-length
  end-if
   move cobol-string (left-spaces + 1:string-length) to varchar-string
   goback
   .
>I believe that both these functions should work on any COBOL platform (that 
>supports Typedef...), but I haven't compiled/debugged them.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>And  around 20 years to get a Standard after 85...

The Standard is moot because no compiler supports it.
```

I coded it like this before posting it, but I changed it to use shifting and 
the already written TrimRight function. I liked the "irony" that the left 
shift actually extends the space padding on the right and this then gets 
knocked off by the TrimRight function.

It really just meant less code. I have come to the belief that shorter 
solutions are better solutions because the less clutter there is, the 
quicker stuff can be found, but I accept that it is arguable and I think in 
the end coding comes down to personal preference in most cases.

Interesting you should think of the above. Coincidentally, yesterday I 
was dabbling and came up with the following (N/E V3.1.) :-

 >---------------------------------------------------------------
method-id. "Begin".

Working-storage Section.
78 TL                   value 50.
01 Table-A.
    05 filler pic x(TL) value "Mary had a little lamb,".
    05 filler pic x(TL) value "Its fleece was white as snow;".
    05 filler pic x(TL) value "And everywhere that Mary went,".
    05 filler pic x(TL) value "The lamb was sure to go.".
01 Table-B redefines Table-A.
    05 TableText occurs 4 pic x(TL).

01 ls-size       pic x(4) comp-5.
01 ls-object     object reference.

01 ws-Text       pic x(TL).
01 n             pic x(4) comp-5.

procedure division.

move length of ws-Text to ls-size
perform varying n from 1 by 1 until n > 4

     move TableText (n) to ws-Text
     invoke CharacterArray "fromBuffer"
         using ls-size, ws-Text
         returning ls-object
     invoke self "Method-2" using ls-object

end-perform

*> The Class notation for the method "fromBuffer" is -

*> The length of the data is calculated by searching backwards
*> from the end of the buffer and finding the last non-space
*> and non-null character.

end method "Begin".
 >---------------------------------------------------------------
method-id. "Method-2".
78 RecLength          value 200.
01 ls-size            pic x(4) comp-5.
01 ls-text            pic x(RecLength).  *> 500, 1,000 OR.... ?

linkage section.
01 lnk-object         object reference.

Procedure Division using lnk-object.

invoke lnk-object "sizeInBytes" returning ls-size

if      ls-size > RecLength
         display "Class - TestChars - Method-2"
         display "Input size greater than receiving Text"
         STOP RUN
end-if

invoke lnk-Object "getValuewithSize"
        using ls-size returning ls-text

*> the four "sizeInBytes" above are 23, 29, 30 and 24

display ls-text (1:ls-size)

End Method "Method-2".
*>--------------------------------------------------------------

What I wanted to do was replace the following type of clumsy "occurs 
from a to b depending upon c" :-

*>---------------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "addToCollection".
*>-------------------------------------------------------------
Local-storage section.
01 ls-string                             object reference.
Linkage section.
01 lnk-record.
    05 occurs 1 to 100
       depending on ws-RecordLength            pic x.

Procedure Division using lnk-Record.

  Evaluate true
    when ofReferences
         invoke CharacterArray "withLengthValue" using
            ws-RecordLength, lnk-record returning ls-string
         invoke os-List "add"
                  using ls-string returning ls-string
    when other
         invoke os-List "add" using lnk-record
  End-Evaluate

End Method "addToCollection".
*>--------------------------------------------------------------

Now in both your 'function' and the snippet for 'addToCollection'
we have the maximum indicated by 'depending on xx' ; in my case above, 
ws-RecordLength I set per collection. However, by using instances of 
Collection 'methods', I wanted something more generic.

In N/E V3.1 I can use the SPECIAL REGISTER -

------------------------------------------------------------------
"LENGTH OF data-name-2 pic 9(09).

"The expression generates a value representing the current number
of bytes of storage used by data-name-2. The expression can be used 
wherever a numeric data item can be used except as a subscript or a 
reference modifier.

It can also be used to set the value of a level 78 item".
-------------------------------------------------------------------

If we go back to my "Method-2" the above becomes :-

method-id. "Method-2".
01 ls-size            pic x(4) comp-5.
01 ls-text            pic x(200).  *> 500, 1,000 OR.... ?
78 RecLength          value Length of ls-text.

but, it doesn't give any mileage.

I note from N/E V 4.0 onwards, that M/F have introduced the ANY LENGTH 
feature - now that might be a possibility - but I would need the 
compiler to confirm how it would be used, particularly as it states it 
is passed through Linkage.

Jimmy

```
On Mon, 08 Dec 2008 20:04:25 -0700, "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote:

>Robert wrote:
>> On Mon, 8 Dec 2008 07:19:38 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
…[147 more quoted lines elided]…
>is passed through Linkage.

ANY LENGTH is ideal for this, because it passes the parameter's length as well as its
address. We wouldn't have to pick an arbitrary input length. A single function could be
called with x(30), x(50) and x(255). 

ANY LENGTH is not a varchar. It can only be used in the linkage section of a called
program or method, indicating the length is determined at execution time. The calling
program must have a prototype so the compiler knows to pass the length. Example:

01  input-30  pic x(30).
01  input-255 pic x(255).

call 'foo' using input-30
call 'foo' using input-255

program-id. foo.
linkage section.
01  input-string pic x any length.
procedure division using input-string.
    display 'Length is ' length of input-string.

The free Fujitsu compiler does not support ANY LENGTH nor prototypes.  One can do the same
by passing the length as a separate parameter, as you did above.
```

Charles Hottel wrote:
> I have recently run across a reason to be interested in these langages and I 
> and IIRC someone here has some experience with at least one of them.  Can 
> anyone recommend any books or web site that they found to be helpful or 
> good?

The official OCaml site[1] has a link to various OCaml tutorials.[2]

I went through the first several parts of the online English
translation of "Developing Applications with Objective Caml".[3] I
thought it was a decent introduction to the language, though I had
experience with other functional languages (including OCaml's ancestor
Standard ML) before I tried it. It's pretty technical and more
focussed on OCaml syntax and features than on the theory behind them.

A quick glance suggests that Didier Rï¿½my's "Using, Understanding, and
Unraveling The OCaml Language"[4], also linked to from [2], might be a
better introduction for someone new to the ML family. It seems to go
into more detail about why OCaml works the way it does, and how best
to use it.

The OCaml documentation page[2] also links to a list of published
books. I've heard good things about Jon Harrop's _OCaml for
Scientists_, but it's expensive.

I've never looked into Haskell resources (just haven't had the time),
but I expect there are online tutorials and the like. Probably lecture
notes too, since it's popular with Computer Science programs.

[1] http://caml.inria.fr/ocaml/
[2] http://caml.inria.fr/resources/doc/index.en.html
[3] http://caml.inria.fr/pub/docs/oreilly-book/index.html
[4] http://caml.inria.fr/pub/docs/u3-ocaml/index.html
```

On 2008-12-04 00:50:12 +0000, "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> said:

> and IIRC someone here has some experience with at least one of them.  Can

I would definitely recommend learning a language like this to broaden 
your mind even if you write code in it for money. Although there is a 
big overlap between Haskell and OCaml so you might be better off 
learning something really low-level like Forth or C instead of _both_ 
of them.

I don't know about OCaml, but I wrote some Haskell in college.

1. Download Hugs from http://haskell.org/hugs/
2a. If you have access to a 3rd-level library, borrow "Haskell: The 
Craft of Functional Programming" by Thompson and read it
2b. Otherwise, work through one of the tutorials on 
http://www.haskell.org/haskellwiki/Tutorials Back in the day, I used 
http://www.haskell.org/tutorial/

In general, haskell.org is an invaluable resource.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: OCaml and Haskell Programming Languages

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-10T13:00:39-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ghp1v701qe8@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<6q8icrFb75hpU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<N_2dnZwup_yitKrUnZ2dnUVZ_sninZ2d@earthlink.com> <6q8icrFb75hpU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> Readers of this thread may find the following also interesting:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> someone who is an ardent OO programmer resisting the advance of paradigms...

I don't think so. Mark Lee Smith's comment was, in essence, that there
have been OO languages with lambda operators and dynamic closures
since Smalltalk (so nearly as long as there have been OO languages at
all), so there is no "advance of paradigms" in this case.

I don't entirely agree - for one thing, I think his claim that
"Smalltalk isn't functional" isn't meaningful in the context of his
larger argument - but the basic point is a good one.
Programing-language "paradigms" like OO and functional are genres,
which is to say they're categories of convenience imposed by
commentators on the gamut of existing languages. They're not
attributes of the languages themselves.

Things like lambda operator, dynamic closures, and higher-order
functions are attributes of a programming language. You can claim that
a language which possess those is "functional", but that's really a
gloss for "you can write functional programs in this language". (And
of course per Kolmogorov you can write functional programs in any
Turing-complete language, so all you're really saying is that it's
convenient to write functional programs in that language.) Saying a
language uses the "functional paradigm" is even more tenuous.

I like functional features in programming languages, and where they're
available I often use them. I like OO features, and I often use them.
But I'm not subscribing to some "paradigm" in either case, and I don't
believe one is advancing toward me brandishing a burning lambda,
either. Yes, there's a trend for languages to pick up functional
features, and that's good. But it doesn't mean they require us to
abandon OO or anything else.

And conversely, it's possible to use at least some functional and OO
patterns in most procedural languages cleanly. I have C programs that
use higher-order functions, polymorphism, and inheritance. No paradigm
shift required; as Lee says, you use tools because they're useful. You
don't have to change teams.
```

## Thread: COBOL/CICS/DB2 - COBOL for MVS and compile option DYNAM - solution from 2006 — 2 this month / 3 total

#### ↳ Re: COBOL/CICS/DB2 - COBOL for MVS and compile option DYNAM - solution from 2006

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-04T08:36:40+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<sEMZk.90183$786.16767@fe11.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<14102947-7263-4caf-bf63-e155606d804b@y18g2000yqn.googlegroups.com>`

```
Are you aware that under CICS when you use the NODYNAM compiler option you can 
still use the
   CALL identifier
       rather than
   CALL "literal"

format so all CALLs to *YOUR* application subprograms are dynamic?  In that 
case, only the CICS/IBM supplied subroutines must be link-edited (bound) into 
your load module.

I can't remember when this was first supported, but I know it has been possible 
for over a decade and a half.
```

<a id="t-c811e7937f"></a>
## Thread: COPY with replacing within copybook is it possible — 1 this month / 2 total

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

_Thread started [2008-11](2008-11.md#t-c811e7937f)._

#### ↳ Re: COPY with replacing within copybook is it possible

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-04T08:40:12+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<MHMZk.84783$Ac1.81133@fe04.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<492FD6C1.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
Yes, it is legal to have REPLACE outside or inside of nested COPY statements (in 
the '02 Standard and as an extension in many - not all - '85 Standard 
compilers) - while mixing COPY/REPLACING with nested copies is not allowed.

There are good (technical) reasons for this having to do with the "required" 
order in which COPY statements are processed.
```

---

<a id="t-3f7b1268f7"></a>
## Thread: COBOL/CICS/DB2 - COBOL for MVS and compile option DYNAM - solution from 2006 — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe) · [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### Re: COBOL/CICS/DB2 - COBOL for MVS and compile option DYNAM - solution from 2006

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-05T11:56:59-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4939170B.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
I think his point is that the SQL precompiler generates CALL 'DSNHLI' and
not CALL DSNHLI, so he would create a dummy DSNHLI object that dynamically
calls the real DSNHLI load module.

One could probably use REPLACE ==CALL 'DSNHLI'== BY ==CALL DSNHLI== insead,
though.

Then again, is it really needed?  If you have a CICS program you are (most
likely) not going to run it in batch, so you can just link in the CICS
version of DSNHLI.  Only for a batch subroutine that will also be called
under CICS would this be an issue.  And there you could, I think, just use
the DYNAM compiler option.

Frank
```

## Thread: It's COBOL, Jim, but not as we know it... — 53 msgs

### It's COBOL, Jim, but not as we know it...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-08T08:08:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6q2la6Fagc56U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I spent a few hours on Sunday enhancing the String2Num component and putting 
it where it can be downloaded.

I've put together a "package" which includes the original white paper I 
wrote on components, with String2Num as a demo, and reviewed and revised the 
whole lot.

If you download this "package" you will obtain the following:

1. A useful component that you can test easily on your own machine. (Web 
pages are provided to allow you to run it locally as a client, or on a local 
server like IIS as server side code. The implications of both are examined, 
and background is provided.) The component can be called from Java, VB, C#, 
C++ and any language which implements the COM interface, including OO COBOL, 
of course. A mainframe version may be made available as well. You are free 
to deploy this component in your own applications if you want to.

2. Examples and explanations (including sample COBOL code).

3. Discussion of why components are NOT modules, and why OO COBOL and OO 
concepts are really worth looking at. (Even if you are working in a 
mainframe environment).

4. A help file that enables you to see at a glance all the methods, 
properties, and events used by the component.

5. What the Component Object Model (COM) is, and how it evolved. Decide for 
yourself why it is important.

6.  A revised and updated version of the original "Components 101 -  A trip 
for COBOL programmers" white paper.

The paper and the component were originally published on the 
"aboutlegacycoding" Web site. Approximately 300 people downloaded the 
component and around 14000 read the white paper. (figures from the web 
master, Buddy Wray.) The current package is better than the original.

GETTING IT:

1. You will be taken to an automatic logon and then redirected to the 
download when logon is successful. (You can cancel out at any time and you 
can remove your registration from my server any time you want to. There are 
two reasons I need you to logon: 1. So I can know who has a copy if I want 
to update/upgrade the component. 2. So I can recognise whether you have 
visited before (your login is an identifier). Beyond that, your email 
address is not used in any way.

2. You will be downloading a Zip file that is around 14 MB. Expect it to 
take around 10 minutes if you have normal broadband. Dial up connections 
should be left overnight...

3. There are two ReadMe1st files in the Zip archive; one .pdf and one .doc. 
The content is identical, but I urge you to read one of them before 
extracting the Zip file.

OK, here's the link: 
http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobdata/security.aspx?SourcePage=x&request=S2N
(This link is ONLY published here in CLC at the moment, but I'll set a page 
on the cobdata server when I get time...)

Please comment on the material, good or bad, and post any observations or 
questions here.

(I'm offering a no-prize to the first person who breaks it or gets a wrong 
result : - ))

Enjoy!

Pete.
```

What Browser? When did you access? Does this ALWAYS happen? Did you actually 
see the login page?

I just clicked the link and it worked perfectly. We have been doing some 
work on the server but it should not affect this.

Here's what SHOULD happen:

1. You click the link in the newsgroup message.(ONLY ONCE... don't double 
click it...)

2. The logon page opens, followed a few seconds later by the cobdata page 
opening in the background. (You should maximize the login page if it isn't 
already.)

3. You login or register. It succeeds. You click "Resume" and the standard 
download dialog for the ZIP file appears.

4. You download. Close the page. The standard cobdata page remains.

(since Sunday, there have been 7 successful downloads.)

Here's what CAN happen:

1. Both the login and COBDATA main page appear simultaneously. This has to 
do with browser delay and/or server workload. If it happened, it shouldn't 
happen consistently. Everything will still work, but it could be confusing 
to a user.

2. The login page may be minimized and all you see is the COBDATA main page. 
This has to do with Browser settings or tabs if you are using a tabbed 
browser.

3. The COBDATA page is obscuring the login page. Minimize the COBDATA page 
and it should reveal the login page.

Here's my comment on what you reported:

1. First off, sorry it didn't work. I can't reproduce the error. This 
doesn't mean I don't believe you... : - ).

2. The "page not found" represents a 404 error code.This is different from 
you not having permission (none should be required), and would normally 
occur if the URL was wrong, or the page had been removed from the site. 
(Neither of these things has happened.) Is there any chance that the string 
following the ?  or the first & have been stripped out or removed from the 
link? Could you try pasting the link into the address bar of your browser?

3. If you got to the login but the download failed, it might be because it 
is a ZIP file, and your browser is not configured to allow this type of 
file, or your firewall is blocking it.

4. I can't envisage circumstances where you would be blocked from access to 
the login file, if you clicked the link provided. Does the "Page not found" 
message reference the login page or the ZIP file?

5. The site uses transient cookies which expire after 24 hours. Can you make 
sure that cookies are enabled for http://primacomputing.co.nz ? If these are 
suppressed, you will not necessarily get a message (I'll look at changing 
this when I get time) and results would just be unpredictable.

The no-prize was really for people who use the component, but I appreciate 
you letting me know you are having access problems with the site.

If none of the above suggestions work, mail me privately and I'll give you 
another pathway to it.

Cheers,

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: It's COBOL, Jim, but not as we know it...

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-09T17:00:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6q68rpFauuruU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6q2la6Fagc56U1@mid.individual.net> <UpqdnYPLP5pd46DUnZ2dnUVZ_sLinZ2d@earthlink.com> <6q5lihFau95hU1@mid.individual.net> <3gcrj4drltsna4sfbksnd7r0061esp2m2i@4ax.com> <6q5tffFb10dfU1@mid.individual.net> <96f9a3ad-4e91-4108-88eb-6bf26274ef1a@l33g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard wrote:
> On Dec 9, 1:46 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[45 more quoted lines elided]…
> _stop_ being infected with ActiveX.

Gosh Richard, perhaps you were ... mistaken?

ActiveX is just code like any other computer code. It can be used for good 
or it can be used for bad.

It is actually no more dangerous than any other kind of script.(An ActiveX 
control has no more permissions on your system than a Java Bean, for 
example.)

Many millions of people find ActiveX components to be useful devices that 
enhance their computer use. Calendars, juke boxes, and very effective online 
virus scans from companies like Panda and Trend are all implemented by means 
of ActiveX controls.

Not everyone writing them is malicious, any more than everyone who drives a 
car is a murderer.

Rather than seeing a system being "infected" with ActiveX, I see the lack of 
support for it in Firefox as an omission. So do the hundreds of thousands of 
Firefox users who have downloaded the add-on.(Which, by the way, is very 
well implemented and simple to download and use...)

Malice, like Beauty, is often in the eye of the beholder.

Pete.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6q8623Fb9kebU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6q2la6Fagc56U1@mid.individual.net> <UpqdnYPLP5pd46DUnZ2dnUVZ_sLinZ2d@earthlink.com> <6q5lihFau95hU1@mid.individual.net> <3gcrj4drltsna4sfbksnd7r0061esp2m2i@4ax.com> <6q5tffFb10dfU1@mid.individual.net> <96f9a3ad-4e91-4108-88eb-6bf26274ef1a@l33g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <6q68rpFauuruU1@mid.individual.net> <6q7jeoFb5o07U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <6q68rpFauuruU1@mid.individual.net>,
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> writes:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> until you run it at which point, it is too late.

That is simply not true. Web based ActiveX components that install to your 
client announce themselves. Download the demo and see for yourself.

> Which is why many
> people (and a lot of organizations) don't allow it.
>

Organizations make blanket decisions that are usually the lowest common 
denominator to protect themselves. You can't really blame them.

Bill, my point was that often people see malice where there is none. (It is 
a form of paranoia.) It is exacerbated by a lack of understanding.

Java beans, ActiveX, JavaScript  all have no more rights on your system than 
you allow them. They cannot overwrite your hard drive like a virus can, for 
example, unless you specifically grant write access rights to them. Given 
that an ActiveX announces itself and you know what it is for and where it is 
coming from, the risk is negligible. (Unless you are a person who sees 
malice everywhere, in which case the risk is enormous... you know they are 
out to get you. : - )) Would you not download a virus scan ActiveX because 
it is an ActiveX? No, you make an informed decision. You know what you are 
getting and the source of it. It serves a very useful purpose.

There is a lot of paranoia. Because the actual technology is a MicroSoft 
one, many people mistrust it for that reason alone.

I don't think people who ban scripts and ActiveXs are wrong; they have a 
right to do whatever they like with regard to their systems, but I do think 
they are missing out on a lot.

Pete.
```

> On Wed, 10 Dec 2008 10:25:21 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> source.

You can achieve this by placing the site in the Trusted zone, and setting
the level to Low. Check out the "Security" tab on the internet options for
your IE browser.



>  They don't even have an option to allow all scripts from a
> given source for the session so I have gotten multiple prompts when
> doing Windows Update.  Also on the home edition, I haven't seen any
> way to configure the permissions for Active X.

Again, it is in the security tab of Internet options. It isn't dependent on
what version of XP you have, it is dependent on the Browser.

To REALLY configure the detailed security for a COM/ActiveX component you
need to go to the "Administrative Tools" option in Control Panel and open
the Component Services tool.  The act of installing a component does NOT run
it... you are able to configure it before it runs. Click down to the "DCOM
config" tab, locate the component in the list, then right click on its entry
and open its properties. This is a tabbed display and the security tab
allows you to decide what userid, authentication, computer on the network,
or anything else you need in order to be secure. To go to these drastic
lengths is more than the average user needs, as the defaults are usually
fine, but you CAN configure a component to run exactly HOW (permissions) and
WHERE (a computer or URL you have run permissions on), and under what
authentication (user or group ID) you want it to.  You can make it as loose
or as tight as you like, and you can even prevent certain users from
accessing it. As a component developer I use this tool all the time and test
stuff with differing levels of security and authentication, but it is not
needed for the average user.

It is ironic to me to see a number of people declaiming ActiveX/COM
technology as "dangerous" when it is actually completely configurable,
stable, and safe. These same people are using this same technology every day
and enjoying the benefits of it, if they run under Windows, whether they
realise it or not.

Most users don't have an Object Browser (that they know about... there is an
excellent one included with VB and VBA, which anyone who has installed MS
Office will have...and an even better one included with Visual Studio...) 
and wouldn't know what to do with it if they did.

It is a pity, because if you were to open one up and tell it to browse for 
COM
controls, you would be surprised at the several thousand it reports
installed on your system. (They do everything from playing music and videos, 
converting text to speeech, providing GUI interface controls, sending email, 
connecting you to Help, running both Internet and Windows Explorers, to 
system oriented tasks like resource management and  monitoring and logging 
system events...Windows would be unrecognisable (and useless) without them).

That is part of the "danger" of course. When you download an Activex 
component it becomes just like an extension of the Operating system (which 
is largely composed of such components) and that is why you should be 
careful about what you download and what permissions you give it.

For a developer, this is a goldmine of re-usable,ready written functionality 
that is available on every Windows system.

You can include it in your applications and/or web pages. BUT, it does tie 
them to Windows unless you take special steps that have more to do with 
remotely running the components (COM+), than using them locally.

Even without the 40,000 classes available in .NET there are several thousand
classes already available through the COM interface, that are simply part of
the Windows environment. If you target Windows machines you can use these
components without royalties or run fees because they are already registered
on the target system. (it is very similar in principle to using the Windows
API ). ActiveX/COM componentry is an inherent part of Windows and the
Windows environment, and looks like remaining so for the foreseeable future.

Pete.
```

After re-reading the above, I thought it might help to provide an example.

Anyone who has MicroSoft Office and/or MS Access installed on their system 
can run the following demo.

Here are 16 lines of standard HTML code. It represents a web page that has 
an ActiveX component embedded in it:

<!DOCTYPE html PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD XHTML 1.0 Transitional//EN" 
"http://www.w3.org/TR/xhtml1/DTD/xhtml1-transitional.dtd">
<html xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">
<head>
<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1" />
<title>Show a Windows ActiveX component </title>
</head>
<body>
Here's an ActiveX component embedded in a Web Page... Could you write this 
functionality in 16 lines of code?
<p>
<OBJECT
CLASSID="clsid:8E27C92B-1264-101C-8A2F-040224009C02"
WIDTH=300 HEIGHT=200 BORDER=1 HSPACE=5 ID=objDemo>
</OBJECT>
</p>
</body>
</html>


You should be able to cut and paste this code into Notepad and save it as an 
HTML file (name it anything .htm or .html)

Double click on it and it will run, if your Browser has been set to allow 
ActiveX controls to run. (medium or low security, for IE).

The actual display depends on how your system is configured, but it is worth 
looking at.

It works in Firefox provided you have the IE Tab add-in installed.

(Get the Firefox add in here: http://ietab.mozdev.org/ )

Having run the HTML code above (including the embedded ActiveX object, which 
lives on your system and was not downloaded from the Web), and found that 
the world didn't end, why not try the String2Num component for validating 
numeric strings?

OK, here's the link:
http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobdata/security.aspx?SourcePage=x&request=S2N

Full details are in the original post to this thread. It is a COBOL 
component.

If enough people download it, I'll post the source code publicly. At the 
moment 12 people have downloaded it; I'd like to see 20... : - )

Pete.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
> For a developer, this is a goldmine of re-usable,ready written
> functionality that is available on every Windows system.

Yes, but.

In the case at issue, converting an alphanumeric string to a number, 
Active-X is a terrible solution. I suspect you've become so enamored of COM 
objects, you've lost sight of some other desirable characteristics.

For example, in the longhand example that Robert offered, his code simply 
ignores X'00' bytes. It's a simple matter, in his code, to take 
extraordinary steps when encountering something that may be prevalent in the 
data (such as a binary zero or space). In a closed component - such as 
Active-X - you can't.

Of course this is one of the nominally swell features of Active-X, but 
contraindicated in the case at hand.
```

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-15T15:53:36+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6qlv5hFcttqiU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6q2la6Fagc56U1@mid.individual.net> <UpqdnYPLP5pd46DUnZ2dnUVZ_sLinZ2d@earthlink.com> <6q5lihFau95hU1@mid.individual.net> <3gcrj4drltsna4sfbksnd7r0061esp2m2i@4ax.com> <6q5tffFb10dfU1@mid.individual.net> <96f9a3ad-4e91-4108-88eb-6bf26274ef1a@l33g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <6q68rpFauuruU1@mid.individual.net> <6q7jeoFb5o07U1@mid.individual.net> <6q8623Fb9kebU1@mid.individual.net> <1d55k4pe7293vevupifchs8n0rckv5hl4h@4ax.com> <6qj61oFcgh39U1@mid.individual.net> <586dnUI-0-iTM9jUnZ2dnUVZ_hydnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> such as Active-X - you can't.

You can. If you have the source you can extend a method or property, if you 
don't have the source you can suggest an improvement to the developer. (Most 
developers are very pleased to hear feedback on their components, and will 
do what they can to accommodate you.)
>
> Of course this is one of the nominally swell features of Active-X, but
> contraindicated in the case at hand.

I think that's just silly.

You are saying write code for every instance of everything, so it can be 
available for modification, should the need arise. (The fact that it is 
available for modification is not conducive to thinking a bit more before 
modifying it...)

Whether you use Java Beans or activeX or any other component technology, the 
idea is to avoid re-inventing the wheel and to have a consistent approach. 
If you would rather write lines of code, then that's your choice.  You are 
committing yourself to maintaining thousands of lines of code and adding to 
the number every year.

That has been the habit of the past, but it is no longer necessary (or 
desirable, in my opinion...)

You may have seen how recently in this forum how sixteen lines of code and a 
component can save hundreds, maybe even thousands of lines of code. And if 
the component is extended, upgraded or replaced there is no impact on 
existing applications. They don't even need to be recompiled.

If you did want to change the behaviour of the component (for example, you 
might want to have blank fields NOT considered to be zero, which the 
component assumes), it is not really hard to check for spaces before 
invoking the component, then not doing so if you don't want zeros. If you 
wanted certain characters handled a certain way you might pre-process for 
them in the same way.

 The function of a component is to do what it does; not do something like 
what it does because you think it should. Perhaps you need to extend it with 
some added functionality; fine, write another component and use them both to 
achieve your aim. You don't modify existing behaviour unless you want to 
reap the whirlwind.

(I realise this may be a foreign concept to people used to working in 
COBOL...)

If you had the source of the component you would be changing it whenever you 
felt there was a need to, and that would defeat the whole object of it.  You 
would then find other programs which used it no longer functioned correctly 
and the result would be what is accepted on most COBOL sites: maintaining 
code will require most of the budget.

(In fact it is totally self-defeating because the act of "fixing" stuff 
causes other breakages...until eventually programmers despair of modifying 
already over-maintained code and the whole thing gets rewritten... or 
maintenance programmers start looking for another job.)

Extending a method or adding a new one is one thing (and for myself, I don't 
even do that unless there is a compelling need to) but fiddling with 
existing behaviour is quite another.

Personally, I don't really care whether you use String2Num or not; it was 
intended to demonstrate a concept. I use it and find it very handy. I have 
done so for 7 years now and never maintained it, apart from recently adding 
the completely new functionality to support floating point. (So, far I'm 
happy to report that none of my applications have broken because I did 
this...)

If you read "Components 101" and still think you'd rather write and maintain 
bespoke code, then a component based approach isn't for you. That's fair 
enough. Your choice.

It isn't about ActiveX, it is about encapsulated functionality vs bespoke 
programming. Mental adjustment is required to move to a component based 
approach. It really is not about technology.

I guess the closest analogy I can think of is when an organization 
introduces SAP or Siebel. People get upset because procedures and processes 
they know and love are overturned. It creates resentment. But the management 
decide that it is cheaper to change some of the Business Processes than it 
is to keep on writing bespoke code (with its ever-escalating costs...), so 
they enforce the new order.

(I can recount from personal experience where a certain manager told me 
(with some degree of glee...):"The package will never be able to deal with 
our pricing plans, they are way too inconsistent  and individual..."(it was 
also costing over a million pounds a year to keep them running in COBOL...). 
Within 6 months customers had been sold standard pricing as a "new deal" and 
it ran flawlessly under SAP. One or two who had struck individual deals with 
particular salesmen threatened to quit, but separate incentives were offered 
instead of complex discounts and everybody calmed down. The net result was a 
significant saving to the company, the streamlining of pricing 
administration, and a fairer deal for all customers. Sometimes, there are 
advantages in standardising functionality.)

Slowly the shop floor adjusts to the new processes required by the package. 
Three years later you ask them if it is any good. They can't imagine life 
without it.

Pete.
```

> existing behaviour unless you want to reap the whirlwind.

Or, in my case, ignore the proffered component because it is non-modifiable.

>

strategic difference between adding space <> zero and adding floating point 
conversions?

floating point stuff in a completely separate module? That way the original

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Active X problems was Re: It's COBOL, Jim, but not as we know it...

_(thread depth: 14)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-16T12:11:20+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6qo6gqFduotaU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6q2la6Fagc56U1@mid.individual.net> <UpqdnYPLP5pd46DUnZ2dnUVZ_sLinZ2d@earthlink.com> <6q5lihFau95hU1@mid.individual.net> <3gcrj4drltsna4sfbksnd7r0061esp2m2i@4ax.com> <6q5tffFb10dfU1@mid.individual.net> <96f9a3ad-4e91-4108-88eb-6bf26274ef1a@l33g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <6q68rpFauuruU1@mid.individual.net> <6q7jeoFb5o07U1@mid.individual.net> <6q8623Fb9kebU1@mid.individual.net> <1d55k4pe7293vevupifchs8n0rckv5hl4h@4ax.com> <6qj61oFcgh39U1@mid.individual.net> <586dnUI-0-iTM9jUnZ2dnUVZ_hydnZ2d@earthlink.com> <6qlv5hFcttqiU1@mid.individual.net> <Lqmdnb8EcsfP89vUnZ2dnUVZ_s3inZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> non-modifiable.

If I posted the source code you still would not use it.


>
>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> the new or different condition.

But it reqirescomplete regression testing of everything that uses the 
subroutine. You are missing the point of encapsulation.

>
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> that could have been just as easily handled by another component?

Yes, I considered the risk and found it acceptable. (I'm pressed for time at 
the moment). However, the correct approach would have been to add a new 
method to the existing component or, indeed to write a new component and 
invoke it when floating point was encountered.  (If I didn't have the source 
I would certainly have done that) Despite what you may think, I do not have 
religious fervour about anything computer related and usually opt for 
pragmatic solutions.


> What's the strategic difference between adding space <> zero and
> adding floating point conversions?

Functional divide and lines of code.

Floating point is a functionality that can reasonably be expected to be part 
of what the component is designed to achieve. It will require more than one 
or even a few lines of code so it should be encapsulated into another 
function. The vehicle for this function could be a new component or it could 
simply be a new method in the existing component. Either way, existing 
encapsulation should not be violated. (I actual fact I added a new section 
to an existing method, which was very wrong of me (I said as much at the 
time...) I "got away with it" because the new section maintains 
encapsulation and is ONLY ever activated if floating point is encountered. 
This makes it very similar to a new method, but without requiring the 
external and internal housekeeping that a new method would. Nevertheless, 
the proper approach would have been a new method. On reflection, I might 
change it to that when I have time.

Changing spaces to zeros or not is a trivial test that doesn't require 
encapsulated functionality.  It can be done easily, external to the 
component. If I was going to modify the component toallow this, it would be 
through a Boolean property that could be set or unset to allow or disallow 
this check. The fact that the component currently defaults spaces to zeros 
is a simply a matter of convenience for the internal code.

>
> Why not check for floating point before invoking the component and
> handling floating point stuff in a completely separate module?

That would certainly be a viable approach. However, the stated purpose of 
the module is to determine whether a string can legitimately be considered 
numeric or not, and floating point is a valid numeric format so it is not 
unreasonable to expect the component to handle it. Besides, Robert threw it 
out as a challenge and I like challenges. :-)


>That
> way the original component would remain unsullied and the chance of
> corrupting hundreds of other modules would be zero? That you would
> have to replicate 2/3rds of the original code is but a small price to
> pay for integrity!

Why would I have to replicate 2/3rds fo the original? Again, you are missing 
the point of encapsulation.
>
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> No, for some, it is a religious experience.

As a devout Atheist, I wouldn't know :-)
>
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> "Come let us gather at the river..."

Sometimes, trying to present what may be new concepts can be seen to be 
evangelizing. If my comments and observations have come across that way, I 
apologize. I really have no vested or other interest in people using 
components. They work for me, saved me a fortune when I migrated away from 
COBOL,  and provide excellent ROI every day. I LIKE trusting other people's 
code and not having source for it. I only have one lifetime. I cannot be all 
things to all people and I cannot write every piece of  functionality I am 
likely to need. (Besides, others have areas of specialization and knowledge 
I couldn't hope to encompass in a dozen lifetimes; I'm glad to have their 
wisdom encpasulated into objects. I trust Windows every day, use some of its 
components to leverage the price I paid for it, and have no source for 
thesecomponents either. Do you think the one I posted as a demo is any less 
useful because I don't have the source and cannot change how it works? 
Neither do I have source for the various third party components I have used 
for years and which are serving me well. I see encapsulation as a VERY 
useful approach, I see re-use and moving away from source code maintenance 
as desirable. Like most f the world, I see the future of computer 
programming in encapsulated objects.

But I totally respect the right of others not to.

As you have not claimed the no-prize, I can only assume you have not tested 
the component, or you couldn't make it fail.

If it is the latter, then I cannot see why you would object to something 
that does what it is supposed to do, does it reliably and well, even adds 
value to the basic requirement by providing alternative formats, yet you 
describe it as a "terrible solution"... Because you are unable to modify it?

What's the difference between "religious fervor" and "not the way we do 
things around here"? :-)

Pete.
```

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <6q68rpFauuruU1@mid.individual.net>,
> 	"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> writes:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> until you run it at which point, it is too late.

It's impossible to prove in the general case that a program does not
have undesirable effects (as that's isomorphic to solving the Halting
Problem). It doesn't matter how the code is delivered.

So this is not a problem with scripting, much less with scripting
languages. It's a problem of trust and restriction of privilege, and
it exists for all software.

>  Which is why many
> people (and a lot of organizations) don't allow it.

Sure. That can be a reasonable response, depending on your threat
model. I don't run any ActiveX controls that aren't required for my
work, and I bolster the default Javascript / JScript sandboxes in
various ways (eg whitelisting scripts in Firefox with NoScript).

But like any question of security, this isn't an absolute. There's no
such thing as "absolutely secure" or "absolutely unsafe". There are
threat models and countermeasures.
```

```
On Tue, 9 Dec 2008 17:00:55 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>ActiveX is just code like any other computer code. It can be used for good 
>or it can be used for bad.

As a programmer, more powerful tools have always been attractive.
Microsoft is full of programmers who have had the same attractions,
and those programmers have created powerful tools.

Occasionally a redesign is necessary to make tools more safe.   Apple
had enough control over its systems that it was able to do that
without much complaining.   Cars, drills, and ladders have been
redesigned for safety.   Microsoft had a harder time as it has worked
to keep compatibility with older programs running under Windows & DOS.

>It is actually no more dangerous than any other kind of script.(An ActiveX 
>control has no more permissions on your system than a Java Bean, for 
>example.)

It sounds as though it has changed, or maybe it has changed under some
versions of Windows, I don't know.   At one time, the Java sandbox
provided more security than Active-X did.

At any rate, even if the reasons no longer apply, I want to make
things easier for my customers, regardless of which environment they
are browsing from.   If they are running Firefox on a Linux machine, I
can assume that they will have Java available.   I cannot assume they
have Active-X available.

Active-X makes things easy for programmers, as long as we're willing
to ignore the wants of our users.   We can complain that the users are
foolish (as security people do when they use passwords in a
non-optimal manner), but it doesn't matter.

> > On Dec 9, 1:46 pm, "Pete Dashwood"

> example.)

That is simply not true. An ActiveX control that has been loaded by a
webpage onto a Windows system is just another executable and has
access to everything that any other program can do, such as writing to
the disk.

A Java applet (which is what can be in a webpage) runs in a sandbox
and cannot write to the disk, or access other processes.

While both can be security risks (especially on Windows) ActiveX is
while working exactly as designed and Java only is when it can exploit
a flaw.

> enhance their computer use. Calendars, juke boxes, and very effective online
> virus scans from companies like Panda and Trend are all implemented by means
> of ActiveX controls.

The mere fact that virus scans are necessary is an indication that the
security model is completely wrong.


> Not everyone writing them is malicious, any more than everyone who drives a
> car is a murderer.

Richard wrote:
> On Dec 9, 5:00 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[63 more quoted lines elided]…
> the disk.

But it can't get to your system via that route unless you enable  and allow 
it. There was a time when IE used to simply download the component 
transparently. That doesn't happen any more. That's why I need for people to 
do a separate install of the component before they can run the demo.

It has to be installed and registered. It has topass authentication and mmay 
have a signed digital signature which guarantees you are getting what you 
thought you were getting. All of this has a bearing on the permissions (like 
file writing) that it ends up with. It can be disabled in seconds by 
de-registering it, unlike a virus, trojan, or backdoor. It is VISIBLE on 
your system.

As someone who has been writing these components for over a decade now I can 
honestly say I have allowed controlled ActiveX downloads to my system and 
have never had a problem with it. Security in the last few years has been 
much improved as Howard alluded to.

>
> A Java applet (which is what can be in a webpage) runs in a sandbox
> and cannot write to the disk, or access other processes.

The "sandbox" is a set of security measures which seriously limit the 
usefulness of applets. So much so that Sun has now chosen to emulate the 
MicroSoft system of signed authentication which is used by ActiveX controls.

http://www.javaworld.com/javaworld/jw-05-1997/jw-05-security.html

>
> While both can be security risks (especially on Windows) ActiveX is
> while working exactly as designed and Java only is when it can exploit
> a flaw.

You could see it that way. I don't. But then, I don't write malicious 
components that do damage while working "exactly as designed".
>
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> security model is completely wrong.

The model is evolving. It is no more "wrong" than any other model would have 
been. Investment of many millions has been made into improving it. Judging 
today's model by what was true yesterday, is simply misleading.

Pete.
```

Java always has had several levels of security. The default is that it
runs in a sandbox. It can, however, and always has been able to,
request increased facility using a signed mechanism.

> http://www.javaworld.com/javaworld/jw-05-1997/jw-05-security.html

Which is dated 1997. Hardly 'Sun has _now_'.
