- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghtno4$hvh$1@reader1.panix.com> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <ghv3ut$2vh$3@reader1.panix.com> <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com> <gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com> <e0kdk4pq6b46660uq2b7shvoc7lrs3ljm4@4ax.com> <gi6slm$1ml$1@reader1.panix.com> <gi94kc2qt9@news2.newsguy.com> <esW1l.439764$5p1.422312@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com> <79ae08ed-86ac-4d6b-b263-253ac141fa15@w1g2000prk.googlegroups.com> <JHY1l.95060$Wo1.27914@en-nntp-04.dc1.easynews.com> <49487642$0$5454$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <256f44c3-7dcd-47f9-9cc2-bb6e391c8421@a26g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <4949bdf8$0$5467$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul wrote:
> On 2008-12-16 22:17:59 -0600, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> said:
>> On Dec 17, 4:47 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> get back a file handle.

I think the problem here is that "handle" is not a word used by the C
standard, nor by the Single Unix Specification. Microsoft does use it
as a technical term, but other people have defined it differently.

The C standard and SUS use the terms "FILE object" and "stream" for
the object pointed to by a FILE*.

Of course some people do call a FILE* a "handle", but I've also seen
"handle" used for Unix/POSIX file descriptors. Mostly the term seems
to be used informally in the Unix world.

> That can vary by operating system, but a POSIX compliant OS will do
> things that way.
> 
> Microsoft is *not* POSIX compliant here.

To be fair, the Microsoft C runtimes have long (always?) included
fopen, etc. Since C90, of course, they're part of the C library and
have to be included by any conforming hosted implementation.

And in the Microsoft C implementation, a FILE* is a pointer to a
struct _iobuf, which is quite like the typical POSIX _iobuf. It refers
to the file using a small integer descriptor. This used to be an index
into the _iob array; these days that array doesn't have an identifier
in userspace, but instead is an anonymous array retrieved via a
function. But the descriptor is still an index, and all of this is in
process memory, again just like in POSIX.

Again, this is Microsoft's C library, which is different (though
implemented on top of) the Windows API.

> Microsoft uses the term "file handle" to refer to a low level construct
> more like a POSIX file descriptor, and then provides some wrapper
> functions to support Microsoft file handles like integers.

That's the Windows API. Windows handles are pointers, and there are
ways to create a C stream from a Windows file handle, or to extract
the file handle from a C stream, so the two APIs can interoperate.

> I understand that Micosoft is working to come into POSIX compliance in
> Windows 7. It was supposed to be in Vista, and is to some extent I think.

Windows has been POSIX-compliant since NT. POSIX support is
implemented through the POSIX subsystem, and hasn't been popular, but
it's there.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: C and Low-level Storage (was: I need a new compiler...

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** Paul <paul-nospamatall.raulerson@mac.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-16T21:41:34-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<494874ee$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghtno4$hvh$1@reader1.panix.com> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <ghv3ut$2vh$3@reader1.panix.com> <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com> <gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com> <e0kdk4pq6b46660uq2b7shvoc7lrs3ljm4@4ax.com> <gi6slm$1ml$1@reader1.panix.com> <gi94kc2qt9@news2.newsguy.com> <esW1l.439764$5p1.422312@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
On 2008-12-16 17:02:31 -0600, "William M. Klein" 
<wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> said:

> <much debate snipped>
> 
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> be "nice to know" - but is different from what actual implementations DO.

If I understand what you are saying Bill, you are asking if C the 
language can access "system memory."

Technically, C can access any address the machine can provide access 
to, including any system storage.

Practially speaking, the Operating System, and sometimes the hardware, 
protects some areas of memory. This is especially true in all our 
modern systems like z/OS, Linux, z/VM, and even Windows, since 
applications run in a designed application space.

For example, on my zLinux systems, every application in the system 
loads and run from  address X'400000'. Of couse, that is a *virtual* 
address, which means there may be a thousand applcations running from 
the virtual address, but from 1000 different real addresses. It gets 
complex, because sometimes text (code) sections are designated 
read-only in memory, and only one copy of them is loaded. (This is 
Linux,
drives z/OS programmers crazy because they cannot store variable in the 
code section - at least if they ever intend to change those variable's 
values... :)

And then you get into shared memory segments. Take the same chunk of 
real memory, attach it to one or more applications - at different 
virtual addresses.

Like I said, it gets complex. Fun though. :)

Another point to consider, most of Unix, including the kernel level 
code (which by the way, usually *does* have access to every real memory 
address, system and application....)  is written in plain old C. Just a 
couple or three thousand lines of assembler, and the rest, millions of 
lines of code, in C.

Just out of curiousity - are there any OS's written in Cobol?
I don't know of any, but I bet there are one or two out there. :)
```

```
On 2008-12-16 22:25:13 -0600, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> said:

> On Dec 17, 4:41ï¿½pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
>> On 2008-12-16 17:02:31 -0600, "William M. Klein"
…[76 more quoted lines elided]…
> assembler) that "*does* have access to every real memory address",

Because I look at the assembler code that the C compiler generates of course.
The following code will generate an error, because memory address 100 
is protected
by MacOS and UNIX.

#include <stdio.h>
int main(int argc, char *argv[]) {

  char *aCharPointer;
  char aCharVariable;

  aCharPointer = 100;
  aCharVariable = *aCharPointer;

  }

In Intel (or Wintel Parlance)

        .text
.globl _main
_main:
        pushl   %ebp
        movl    %esp, %ebp
        subl    $24, %esp
        movl    $100, -16(%ebp)
        movl    -16(%ebp), %eax
        movzbl  (%eax), %eax
        movb    %al, -9(%ebp)
        leave
        ret
        .subsections_via_symbols

Easier to see in mainframe GAS assembler I suppose:

        .file   "test.c"
.text
        .align  4
.globl main
        .type   main, @function
main:
.LFB3:
        stm     %r11,%r15,44(%r15)
.LCFI0:
        ahi     %r15,-112
.LCFI1:
        lr      %r11,%r15
.LCFI2:
        st      %r2,96(%r11)
        st      %r3,100(%r11)
        lhi     %r1,100
        st      %r1,104(%r11)
        l       %r1,104(%r11)
        mvc     108(1,%r11),0(%r1)
        lhi     %r1,0
        lr      %r2,%r1
        lm      %r11,%r15,156(%r11)
        br      %r14

The line I bolded above is the one that is doing "aCharVariable = 
*aCharPointer;"
A moments study will show that R1 has the value 100 in it, so in HLASM, 
it would
look like this:

LHI   R1,100
LA    R2,aCharVariable
MVI  0(R2),0(R1)

It is the same on Linux, VMS, AIX, MacOS, and so on, with varying 
degrees of complexity.

By the way, MVI 0(R2),0(R1) is using indirect memory references; in 
essence R1 and R2 are pointers.


> or
> indeed that it is the C code that puts the machine into the mode that
> allows access by real memory address, sans which it cannot be done.

Whether the C code is *allowed* permission to access a particular 
memory address or not has nothing to do with C, it has to do with 
whether or not the Operating System will allow the running application 
to do so.

Code running in Kernel mode is absolutely no different from code 
running in application mode; it merely has different permissions. Of 
course, kernel mode code can access all the memory in the machine it is 
running on (or in the virutal machine it is running on in the case of 
z/VM, VMWare, Parallels, etc.)

One of the jobs of an operating system is to manage memory...


> 
> 
> 
>> Just out of curiousity - are there any OS's written in Cobol?
>> I don't know of any, but I bet there are one or two out there. :)
```

```
On Dec 18, 4:32 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-16 22:25:13 -0600, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> said:
>
…[83 more quoted lines elided]…
> by MacOS and UNIX.


> #include <stdio.h>
> int main(int argc, char *argv[]) {
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>   }

No, it is not the 'operating system' that causes the error, it is the
hardware. Your code is not attempting to access real address 100, it
is only attempting to access an address within the virtual machine
created for your program. Given that Intel is little-endian the
pointer has some segment reference that does not exist within that
virtual machine.

On a Z80 or 8086 a pointer to 0:100 would actually access the real
address 100, but that was a 30 years ago.

In protected mode the machine is _not_ a Z80 or 8086. You cannot
simply set a pointer to an arbitrary value and expect it to access
some data. Pointers are segment:offset and the only valid segments
that you can use without the hardware dumping you are the ones allowed
in your virtual machine.


> In Intel (or Wintel Parlance)
>
…[63 more quoted lines elided]…
> to do so.

No. Wrong. In protected mode it is the hardware that will prevent you
accessing arbitrary addresses. Even then these would be virtual
addresses and not the real address that you think you can get at.

And, has been pointed out before, it is the _implementation_ that
creates the code that runs in protected mode.


> Code running in Kernel mode is absolutely no different from code
> running in application mode; it merely has different permissions.

Actually the 'permissions' can allow special machine instructions to
be executed and, if the compiler can generate these then the code _is_
different from that running in application mode.



> Of
> course, kernel mode code can access all the memory in the machine it is
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
PR wrote:
> 
> Are you by some chance thinking that the hardware controls whether it
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> mode* is used.

Er - most modern CPUs with memory management do implement access rings
in hardware. The OS tells it when to switch (using privileged
instructions), of course, but access can be, and often is, enforced by
the CPU and MMU.

> By the way, under Windows, that *does* access physical memory address
> 100. Applications are ran in a flat memory model, and an absolute
> address, like the one I entered, actually does map to the same
> physical address.

Modern versions of Windows are paged virtual memory systems, and for
an ordinary process, there's no guarantee that any physical page is
mapped to the page slot that contains virtual memory address 100. (By
convention, Windows maps a page at the lowest virtual address, which
will include virtual address 100, but it doesn't *have* to.)

> You really don't understand how an OS works do you?  I recommend you
> go find an old copy of Tennnebaum's MINIX book.

Not terribly relevant for a modern OS. For that matter, even a book as
old as Bach's _The Design of the UNIX Operating System_ would correct
some of these misconceptions. (See the discussion of the page table in
6.2, pp 153-156.)
```

```
On Dec 19, 5:26 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 18, 12:46 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[190 more quoted lines elided]…
> mode* is used.

Certainly the OS is in control of what goes on. In many cases it is
the hardware that will do address translation and checking
permissions.The exception may well be passed back to the OS to deal
with but the OS is not usually involved with examining every CPU
cycle. (In fact that would require CPU cycles to do so, which it would
have to examine, which ...)


> By the way, under Windows, that *does* access physical memory address
> 100. Applications are ran in a flat memory model, and an absolute
> address, like the one I entered, actually does map to the same
> physical address.

You are claiming that the address translation tables do not do
anything.

If the program has legitimate access to a specific address then where
that lies in physical memory is determined by the ADT. In particular
two programs accessing what they think are the same address (ie
identical bit patterns in the pointer) will access completely
different physical memory locations because they have different ADTs.

So while your code may well try to access 'absolute address 100' where
that lies in _physical_ memory depends entirely on where the page
manager has loaded it for your process and the ADT will translate that
access to that page.


> The variable I defined, aCharVar, may be at *offset
> zero in the data segment*, but the virutal address will be wherever
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> No it isn't. It is the same code.

It is only "the same code" if the compiler is capable of generating
that code, and that depends on the particular _implementation_.

> Whether it MAY execute or not has
> nothing to do with
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> the application level code at a higher privilege level.

You may well be able to -in-assembler- but, getting back to the actual
topic, how do you get a particular C compiler to "write those
instructions into application level code" if there is no statement
that the compiler can use to tell it that.


>
> Indeed - what do you think system calls are?

In some systems 'system calls' are requests for the operating system
to perform some function on behalf of the user process. The system
call puts the request on one of the queues and suspends the process
(or not if it is async). The OS processes the request and then puts
the user process back to ready state so it can continue on some later
context switch.

Other systems may do it in other ways.


> > > Of
> > > course, kernel mode code can access all the memory in the machine it is
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

- **From:** Paul <paul-nospamatall.raulerson@mac.com>

If it quacks like a duck and it acts like a duck - it ain't no armidillo.

equivalent
of what you are saying. Of course we assume that an OS runs on a computer,
and uses the facilities of said computer, be it CPU, MMU, or whatever else,
with some effect.



> 
> 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> anything.

I am not saying any such thing. I am saying that low memory is mapped 
to physical memory
in Windows, most Unix, and most mainframe operating systems.

That means to, address what you wrote below, that address X'100 in one 
program is pointing to the same
absolute memory address as X'100' in another program. No, it doesn't 
*have* to, but in the specific cases I
used as examples, it DOES.


> 
> If the program has legitimate access to a specific address then where
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> that code, and that depends on the particular _implementation_.

You think we use a different compiler to write operating systems?
We don't.

I pointed out that a very small percentage of the UNIX kernel is 
written in assembler,
and that is one of the reasons why. Not by the way, because you can't 
do it in C.
Most versions of C allow inline assembler code. :)


> 
>> Whether it MAY execute or not has
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> that the compiler can use to tell it that.

Well, there are several ways, including just writing in-line assembler.
But most commonly, you add a flag to the compiler to tell it to generate
certain code patterns in certain situations.

In some machines - you simply change the PSW - which is accessed at a
particular physical memory address.

I can point you to real examples in a UNIX or Linux kernel if you are really
interested.

> 
> 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> Other systems may do it in other ways.

I was thinking more along the line of service calls SVC's in the mainframe.
In a lot of cases, they are triggered by interrupts. There are as many ways
to write a service call as there are to pluck a duck I suppose.

The point is you use service calls (or the equivalent) in an Operating System
to give application level code controlled access to protected 
instructions and capabilities.

```
On Dec 19, 3:30 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-18 13:03:40 -0600, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> said:
>
…[238 more quoted lines elided]…
> of what you are saying.

In what way is your nonsense 'equivalent' ? How do you derive this
from what I said ?

Your claim was that the OS will prevent access to specific addresses.
While the OS may well set up the address translation table it is the
hardware (CPU + MMU) that will actually prevent an illegal access.


> Of course we assume that an OS runs on a computer,
> and uses the facilities of said computer, be it CPU, MMU, or whatever else,
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> used as examples, it DOES.

Which, however, is irrelevant to the general case.

Your calim was that: "an absolute address, like the one I entered,
actually does map to the same physical address".

Now you say that this only applies to a very small number of specific
addresses in some operating systems.

But, in fact, there is no assurance that the page containing those
small number of 'absolute address' is actually at that physical
address. That page _might_ be at any place in physical RAM and the ADT
will map it. In fact it may even be swapped out and be remapped to a
different physical address later.


> > If the program has legitimate access to a specific address then where
> > that lies in physical memory is determined by the ADT. In particular
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> read more »
```

```
On Dec 19, 4:35 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-18 21:12:25 -0600, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> said:
>
…[263 more quoted lines elided]…
> You think computers run without software or something?

When the program attempts to execute an instruction that accesses an
arbitrary address, say, address 100 then on a real mode machine the
access will occur, on a protected mode machine where this address is
not allowed then it is the hardware (eg CPU/MMU/..) that detects that
the access is invalid.

The OS is not looking at every memory access made by the program and
determining whether it is allowed or not, the hardware is.

While the OS does create the ADT which forms the basis of what is
_allowed_ it is the hardware that uses these to detect when an access
is _outside_ those allowed and will prevent this.




> >> Of course we assume that an OS runs on a computer,
> >> and uses the facilities of said computer, be it CPU, MMU, or whatever els
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> read more »
```

```
On Dec 18, 8:30 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> I am not saying any such thing. I am saying that low memory is mapped
> to physical memory
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> used as examples, it DOES.


In Windows, at least the NT branch, virtual addresses 0x0 - 0x10000
are never mapped* to anything.  This helps catch null pointer
dereferences and similar errors.

Many *nix have a similar policy, although they vary greatly.  Linux,
for example reserves (and doesn't map*) a single page at the low end
of the address space on all architectures that I'm aware of.

It is, of course, quite common to have much/all of the kernal area
mapped into every address space.  In Linux and Windows, for example,
the high one or two GB (on 32 bit machines) are so mapped, but
typically with permissions that prevent the applications for accessing
them, except in limited ways.

Note that unlike zArch, there is *nothing* special about the first
page of memory, physical, virtual, or otherwise, in x86, at least once
you get out of real mode.


*It’s not impossible that in at least some versions those areas are
mapped to actual physical pages, but without and permissions that
would allow them to be accessed  - but that’s an internal detail for
the OS, to aid in tracking page table usage.  In fact I know of (a
different) OS that did just that.  Which doesn’t matter – the
application will still fault when it tries to access those pages.
```

- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghtno4$hvh$1@reader1.panix.com> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <ghv3ut$2vh$3@reader1.panix.com> <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com> <gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com> <e0kdk4pq6b46660uq2b7shvoc7lrs3ljm4@4ax.com> <gi6slm$1ml$1@reader1.panix.com> <gi94kc2qt9@news2.newsguy.com> <esW1l.439764$5p1.422312@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com> <494874ee$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <cf3ec59f-e12b-4a14-adec-1caaf688b5df@b41g2000pra.googlegroups.com> <Vg82l.14609$ZP4.282@nlpi067.nbdc.sbc.com>`

```
On 2008-12-17 08:45:41 -0600, "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> said:

> Seems to me instead of dicking around trying to access information by either
> real, virtual or imagined address, someone should take a timeout and ask,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> way, anyway.

LOL! Good point, and well taken.

I guess the reason is that there are things you simply cannot do in a 
computer unless you have access to the address of the data/code you are 
trying to manipulate. There are many more things you cannot do 
efficiently without that access.

And under the covers, all our compilers are basically doing is moving 
data around from place to place, memory to registers, memory to memory, 
etc. (Assuming of course, the machine has registers. :)

Even COBOL has the ADDRESS OF capability built into it, and COBOL is 
probably the least likely language to need address pointers I can think 
of. Well, outside of SmallTalk and a few others.

-Paul
```

```
On Dec 18, 7:37 am, "Michael Mattias" <mmatt...@talsystems.com> wrote:
> "Paul" <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote in message
>
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
> mmatt...@talsystems.com

MMM- well- another author - that would be *me* has written for the
past 25 years that a program that gives the correct answers but takes
too long to do the job is worthless.

Also written, and taught, that programs that give correct answers, but
take an inordinate amount of resources to do so are not cost effective
in most business.

You can define resources anyway you want, it still comes out the same
way. If it takes too many programmer hours to implement or maintain,
it isn't worth it. Or too much DASD, or too high a license cost. You
*cannot* take a mono-centric viewpoint of "this is the way it has to
be" in terms of software, database, or network engineering, and you
*cannot* ignore the hardware that the software runs on. Not unless you
want to spend half your time justifying why the project does not work,
is over budget, or works but isn't fast enough.

And this is a very different discussion that is C Pointers point to
memory, which is where this started.

-Paul


As for the kinds of things I was talking about, perhaps you have never
written operating systems or operating system code. Think in terms of
task/thread/process control, file systems, indexed file systems,
network transmissions, communications over any kind of a data bus, and
so forth and so on.
```

> Z80 or a PDP-11, it may not be true for other machines.

The term "SYSTEM STORAGE" to a mainframer is equivalent to saying "RAM" 
to a PC person. It only takes on other meanings in the context of an OS 
or runtime environment.

Any kind of memory storage, by the way, has to be included at an 
address the machine can provide access to.

> 
> 
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> execute access.

Technically, they are all just storage allocations - including the 
executable code.
I was speaking specifically of z/OS however, where the code section is 
not (usually) write protected.

I don't think I have ever encountered a system where the code section 
was not readable. Not sure how it would execute it if it could not read 
it.

Seen lots of cases where people put executable code in the data 
sections though. Annoying as that is.


> 
> 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…

<amusement> Since the days of Von Neuman, executable code has been
stored in addressable memory. That memory doesn't
have to be writable - as in the case of ROM memory, but it does have
to be addressable.

Do you have a real instance of a machine where the executable code in
memory is not readable?

```
PR wrote:
> 
> <amusement> Since the days of Von Neuman, executable code has been
> stored in addressable memory.

But not always.

> Do you have a real instance of a machine where the executable code in
> memory is not readable?

There are plenty of 8-bitters that cannot treat code as data - ie, it
can only be read for execution. And 8-bitters still represent
90-something percent of all computers sold in a given year.

DSPs are also usually Harvard-architecture machines (separate
instruction and data).

General-purpose computers are only a tiny segment of the overall
computer market. The most popular computer application in the world,
by far, is "digital clock", and you don't need a 64-bit processor for
that.
```

```
On Dec 18, 8:44 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> I know of no modern digital machines that are not stored program
> machines. Even most embedded processors qualify as GPDCs these days.
>
> Do you have a specific instance of a machine like you are talking
> about? The closest examples I can thing of are some analog computers.



Consider the ubiquitous 8051 microcontroller.  Data memory and program
memory are physically totally separate.  And while there are
instructions that allow you to read from program memory (so you can
store lookup tables and whatnot in the program ROM), those
instructions are completely separate from the ones that you use to
access RAM.  Some versions of the 8051 provide a back door to write
the program ROM, but that's for things like downloading updated
firmware.

Many small controllers follow that model, and some even have different
word sizes for data and program memory.  Consider the PICs - of which
billions (literally) have been produced.  The PIC16s, for example,
have 8-bit wide RAM, and 14-bit wide program memory (other PIC
families vary - the PIC17s, for example having 16-bit program memory).

Higher end controllers do typically follow the more conventional Von
Neumann architecture in that both instructions and data are in a
single address space, rather than separate ones.
```

```
On Dec 19, 3:44 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-18 12:01:59 -0600, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> said:
>
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> I am not sure I understand what you are trying to say here.

That is because you fail to follow the discussion but conflate and
inflate it in meaningless ways. You also generalise the specific and
specificise the general.

The discussion started started when a program could not write to
variables in its own code segment, but the claim was that it could
read these as constants.

I pointed out that with some systems a program may not even be able to
read its own code segment.

You then thought that this meant that _nothing_ could read it.

Do try and keep up.

> Usually, a
> CPU has a an instruction fetch unit that reads the instruction data
> from a memory address. That alone says to me that the instruction data
> space has to be readable. But perhaps you are talking about something
> entirely different.

But, in 'protected mode' the code segment may be _protected_ from
other processes _and_ even from its own process. That is any segment
(in a particular system) may be marked as 'not readable', 'read-only'
or 'read-write', but it may have another protection flag and and that
is 'executable' or 'non-execuatable'.

The 'instruction fetch unit' only cares about the 'executable' flag,
the _data_ fetch/put unit cares about the read/write flags.

How hard is this stuff ?

>
> A lot of machine have separate instruction and data spaces; when I say
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> about? The closest examples I can thing of are some analog computers.

Your knowledge is not a limitation on the types of machines that can
be built.

It is quite easy to imagine an 8bit machine where the instruction
fetch unit accesses, say, a 64Kb ROM and the data fetch/put unit
accesses a completely different 64Kb RAM.

That would mean that the only way of 'loading' a binary program is to
put it in a ROM on some other machine and swap it, but that is how
many embedded systems work anyway. Having the ROM and RAM in the same
address space would simply limit the total to 64Kb rather than each
having a full 64Kb available.
```

      PERFORM UNTIL All-Done

        MOVE Code-instruction(current-instruction)
                 TO CPU
        EXECUTE CPU
     END-PERFORM

As I have already said, there is only one with a different perspective

While the instructions of a program may direct the CPU to operate in
particular ways 'the CPU' is _not_ 'the program'.

The program does not have to feed the next instruction into the CPU
(though it may specify where that is with a branch instruction) that
is the function of the 'instruction fetch' hardware.


> But unassuming clear logic like that will confuse you I bet.

What does 'confuse' is the way that you conflate 'program' and 'CPU'
as if they are the same thing.

"""Which is a silly statement. Of course it [the program] has to read
its own code segment, otherwise it cannot execute it."""

That statement of yours is nonsense, the CPU is _not_ the program. The
CPU can still execute the code even if the program is prevented from
executing an instruction that copies one of those instructions into a
data variable (for example).


In fact you seem so stuck in your perspective that 'the program' and
'the CPU' are the same thing that the examples seem to make no
impression on you.


> So let me
> cover some of the unrelated and silly things you are liable to bring
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> CPU.

'Control of the CPU' (using your terms) is _not_ 'being the CPU' as
your statements seem claim.

A driver may have control of the engine in a car but that doesn't mean
that he has to be able to suck the petrol from the gas tank.

```
Richard wrote:
> On Dec 20, 11:27 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
>> On Dec 19, 12:11 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>>>> <mwoj...@newsguy.com> said:
<snip>

>> You only call it 'nonsense' because you fail to understand it.
>>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> having difficulty.

As an observer of this discussion, I have to say that I don't believe you 
have "twisted" anything. Your analogy of the  car driver not having to suck 
the petrol was a very good one.

And yet, I would ask you this Richard:  Do you honestly believe that Paul 
doesn't know or understand how a program works?

The idea of a program doing instruction fetches is not so far from the truth 
(even though it is not exactly what happens) because if there were no 
program there would be no need for instruction fetching.  However, if we 
substitute "causing" for "doing" in the previous sentence the meaning 
becomes clearer.

On the other hand, if you intend to discuss the mechanics of how 
instructions get fetched and placed in an ALU, it is probably necessary to 
agree that level of discussion before assuming it.

The difficulties here seem to be in the exactitude of  the terminology and 
how it is used. Is there such a wide divide between the following?:

1. "The driver causes the engine to consume fuel". (It's certainly true that 
the engine won't consume fuel unless the driver switches it on...)
2. "The driver causes fuel to be delivered to the engine."  (We know it is 
the fuel pump that does this, but that is implicit in the statement. The 
fuel pump won't be activated unless the engine is switched on.)
3. "When the driver starts the engine, fuel is delivered to it via a 
carburettor or fuel injection system, fed by a fuel pump."

The fact that we may prefer the third statement, does not make the other two 
"wrong".

 I believe Paul feels you are twisting what he says because he is saying 
things that he expects to be interpreted in a normal way and not 
pedantically to the finest level of detail.

You like exactness and interpret his statements in a much tighter way than 
he intended. That isn't wrong, but it isn't necessarily good communication 
either.

(Because of the exact nature of computer programming, most of us here have a 
tendency to do this. It frequently generates heat in this forum. None of us, 
even the ones like me who are very careful about the use of words, can be 
absolutely exact all of the time. We all say things which other people 
interpret differently than we intended, and get miffed because it seems that 
people are deliberately twisting what we say. I believe the Americanism is: 
"Hey, cut me some slack here..." ... which may be construed by some as an 
apology for inaccuracy... )

That's what prompted my question to you. If you stand back and look at the 
discussion you will find two very competent and experienced people who 
certainly know what they are talking about, arguing to the point of 
hostility about things which both of them fully understand.

Two eagles fighting for a cockroach.

I don't believe Paul needs an exposition on the difference between code 
protection and execution protection, (any more than I needed one on 
Relational Databases recently... :-)),  and I don't honestly believe you 
would think that necessary if you stopped and looked at who you are 
responding to. Spelling it out for a Newbie is good; doing it for someone 
with decades of experience is unnecessary and simply creates antagonism.

The sad thing here is that the two of you will probably resent each other 
for some time to come because of the tone of the exchanges, when in fact, if 
you were both working on a project together you'd  probably afford each 
other respect and get along famously.

Respect.

Pete.
```

```
On Dec 20, 2:03 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Richard wrote:
> > On Dec 20, 11:27 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> doesn't know or understand how a program works?

I think that he understands far too well. I can visualise him over an
assembler listing pointing his pencil at each line in turn and
actualising the bit patterns in his brain.

I think that he does consider the program and his mental processing of
the bit patterns as the same thing. I think that he is is so ingrained
with this image that he completely fails to be able to separate these
into any other perspective.


> The idea of a program doing instruction fetches is not so far from the truth
> (even though it is not exactly what happens) because if there were no
> program there would be no need for instruction fetching.

Interestingly some processors do 'run' without there being a program
in memory, it is just that nothing useful results. With a completely
zero memory, the fetches are done, the 'instruction' is decoded and
executed, the result depends on the particular CPU.


> However, if we
> substitute "causing" for "doing" in the previous sentence the meaning
> becomes clearer.

I disagree with either statement. The CPU does fetches without 'the
program' doing or causing _anything_ there is no 'get next
instruction' in the program, that is entirely in the CPU. There is no
'increment the instruction pointer', granted the program does contain
'set instruction pointer to ..' instructions.

In fact, in many cases, the program has much less control than
claimed. When a hardware interrupt occurs, such as a clock tick, then
there is no 'program' that causes the instruction fetch switch to some
other location.

As it happens there may well be systems, process control perhaps,
where a hardware interrupt does not cause a switch, but merely sets a
flag that 'the program' will detect on tits next state cycle.

It may well be that is what Paul considers, and it may be that is
_all_ he knows of.


> On the other hand, if you intend to discuss the mechanics of how
> instructions get fetched and placed in an ALU, it is probably necessary to
> agree that level of discussion before assuming it.

Paul would probly still claim IO was twisting things.

> The difficulties here seem to be in the exactitude of  the terminology and
> how it is used. Is there such a wide divide between the following?:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> "wrong".

Maybe, but Paul was saying the equivalent of 'engine won't run if the
driver can't suck up the fuel'.


>  I believe Paul feels you are twisting what he says because he is saying
> things that he expects to be interpreted in a normal way and not
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> either.

'Poor communication' is when the discussion is described as 'C
accessing memory' when it is about a much smaller subset of that.
Anyone can 'win' by misrepresenting what the discussion is about.

"programs" which activate all of this stuff. (and here I'm using "programs" 
very loosely, to include the OS, the firmware, and the applications. I think

to know:

1) A man must know his limits.  The Craftsman knows that he is not an 
Artist, the Artist knows he is not a Wise One, the Wise One knows... that 
he does not know.

2) A man must work for improvement, for himself and for others.  The 
Craftsman works so that some day he may be an Artist, the Artist works so 
that some day he will be a Wise One and the Wise One works with other Wise 
Ones, knowing that the way you keep a piece of metal sharp is by, at 
times, rubbing it against a harder piece of metal.

3) There are *always* more than two things to know.'

A little lengthy for the average fortune-cookie, I know... maybe there's a 
market for multi-layer fortune cakes?

```
On 2008-12-19 00:11:29 -0600, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> said:

> On Dec 19, 5:51ï¿½pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
>> On 2008-12-18 21:45:45 -0600, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> said:
…[54 more quoted lines elided]…
> again, it was 'low-level access' or 'access to system memory'.

No- it was whether or noe C pointers are always pointers to memory. The 
distiniction of "low level access" only makes a difference from a high 
level language that supports objects or some other higher level 
abstraction.

Access to "system memory" is different from access to "system storage", 
and the term can change in the context you use it.

However, it makes no difference. C can and does access any memory 
exactly the same way. If there is something there to deny access to 
that memory, it is not an inherent property of the C language, it is a 
property of the system the C program is running on.

Again, I doubt you will be able to understand that, as your entire 
agenda appears to not be rational discourse, but rather to prove to 
everyone how much of an ass you can be.

I'll go back to lurking around for another couple years or so.

-Paul

…[36 more quoted lines elided]…

On Dec 19, 5:28 am, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 18, 1:08 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[124 more quoted lines elided]…
> to be addressable.

You appear to conflate various terms and also to inflate the scope of
an argument beyond its actual bounds.

The issue was whether, on a particular system, a user program had read
access to its own code. Of course it is always readable by the
hardware, of course it is always 'addressable' by _something_.


> Do you have a real instance of a machine where the executable code in
> memory is not readable?

Yes I do: the ICL 2900 Series. "A code segment can be marked as
'execute only' and can be protected from accidental writing or reading
within the process. Data segments can be marked as 'data' (to prevent
accidental execution) and as either 'read only' or 'read and write'."

"The ICL 2900 Series", J.K.Buckle. 1978.

On Dec 19, 3:57 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> On 2008-12-18 13:21:12 -0600, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> said:
>
…[171 more quoted lines elided]…
> other words?

Your statement "That memory doesn't have to be writable - as in the
case of ROM memory, but it does have to be addressable." appeared to
be contradicting the statement that executable code only needs to be
readable by the CPU (and not the program).

If you weren't conflating 'readable' and 'addressable' then why did
you make that statement which otherwise seemed obvious and
unnecessary ?



> >> Do you have a real instance of a machine where the executable code in
> >> memory is not readable?
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Do you have any information on how this worked?

Well, yes, I obviously have "The ICL 2900 Series", J.K.Buckle. 1978.
Did you not notice that ? I do have many other papers on ICL machines
too, including others on 2900.

> I confess, I cannot
> imagine how a computer could deal with execute only access to a stored
> program.

Perhaps you failed to read where it said "within the process". As I
said before, you generalise from the specific. The specific case was
'a program reading constants from the code segment', you then seem to
generalise this to 'a (the whole) computer'.

Perhaps it isn't obvious to you that 'the program' which is prevented
from reading its own code segment is only a part of 'a computer', and
that other parts can have different access permissions. For example
the demand page unit _can_ read and write code segments and this unit
isn't "within the program".


> Branching alone would seem to present a challenge, never mind
> things like instruction pipelining and so forth.

Why ?  What is difficult to understand about this ? Why would
_a_program_ need to read its own code segment ?

Just so you don't get confused again and to clarify;

Why would a program need to do: 'MOVE xyz TO some_variable', where xyz
was in the code segment, in order to do a branch ?


> Is this a modern machine?

You should have got a clue from the '1978' as the publication date. It
was however _very_ modern and advanced for its time.

> The only time I remember hearing much about
> ICL anything was I *think* as the GPDC installed about Brit Nimrod ASW
> planes. But I thought that had an IBM 360 instruction set, and that was
> three decades ago.

The ICL System 4 (inherited from English Electric-Leo-Marconi) was a
licence built Spectra 70 and so was IBM 360ish. This pre-dated the
2900 series by a decade or so.

```
On Dec 17, 9:38 pm, Paul <paul-nospamatall.rauler...@mac.com> wrote:
> I don't think I have ever encountered a system where the code section
> was not readable. Not sure how it would execute it if it could not read
> it.


For machines that support non-readable/executable segments, it's just
that the instruction fetch mechanism has different permissions than
the data read mechanism.  It's not different in principal than the
many systems that support (and use) non-executable data sections -
although then the reverse applies - it's the instruction fetch
mechanism that doesn't have permission to read the area of memory.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: C and Low-level Storage

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-18T12:37:07-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gie3b932554@news4.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<494874ee$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <4941e558$0$5493$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghtno4$hvh$1@reader1.panix.com> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <ghv3ut$2vh$3@reader1.panix.com> <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com> <gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com> <e0kdk4pq6b46660uq2b7shvoc7lrs3ljm4@4ax.com> <gi6slm$1ml$1@reader1.panix.com> <gi94kc2qt9@news2.newsguy.com> <esW1l.439764$5p1.422312@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com> <494874ee$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul wrote:
> 
> Technically, C can access any address the machine can provide access to,
> including any system storage.

There's no requirement that a C implementation use the native address
type of the underlying machine for its pointers. There are many cases
where it does not.

A 32-bit C implementation running on a 64-bit system can't "access any
address the machine can provide access to". There are many such
implementations, since most of the general-purpose 64-bit machines
still support 32-bit C programming. There are various ones for
Windows, assorted Unixes, etc.

On the original RIOS RS/6000s (which I worked on before and after they
went GA, when I was with IBM), the machine address space was 56-bit,
but C (and all other languages, including machine code) used a 32-bit
address type. The additional bits were provided by segmentation via
the MMU. Privileged code (ie, the kernel) could effectively address
the entire address space by manipulating segments, but ordinary
processes couldn't, and none of them had a primitive type that
represented it.

And so on.
```

```
William M. Klein wrote:
>   I can't tell.  Have you been debating a "real-world" issue or a philosophical 
> one?
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> program" to system-level storage?

Yes. I mentioned some in the post you're following up on.

> I am NOT a C programmer, but when I have interacted with those that do use it, 
> it seems to me that all implementations that they use DO allow the C program to 
> access (at least "read") data at "system controlled" (low-level?) storage.

I'm not sure what that definition would cover. A great many C programs
execute in virtual storage and cannot read data at arbitrary locations.

> If there are C implementations that do not allow programmers to "read" the 
> storage where "system control blocks, etc" reside, I would be interested in 
> which C and on what platforms these exist.

Pretty much any C program written for a hosted implementation on a
virtual-memory system. That includes most modern general-purpose
computers.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: I need a new compiler...

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-16T15:41:52-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gi94kb1qt9@news2.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<494095a5$0$5502$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <ghufmm41br2@news1.newsguy.com> <ghv3ut$2vh$3@reader1.panix.com> <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com> <gi6ijn$a0h$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <gi63hm324ng@news7.newsguy.com>,
> Michael Wojcik  <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> --end quoted text

Note it says nothing about any correspondence between such a reference
and "low-level memory". As defined by the specification of the C
language, it is a reference to a location in the virtual machine. In a
particular C implementation, it might be a reference to a physical
memory location (as in many small systems), or to a location in
process virtual memory (as in many general-purpose systems), or to an
object in system-wide virtual store (as in the capability architecture
used by IBM's iSeries, formerly AS/400, line).

Or it could be a magic token that summons an invisible fairy who
fetches the data from a mushroom in the forest. The definition of the
language *leaves this up to the implementation*.

> Now... if the reference provided by a pointer is one towards low-level 
> memory (a sub-set of 'anywhere')

If it is. But it may not be, since there are many other subsets of
"anywhere". The quoted text doesn't say that pointers could be
constructed to point to anywhere; it says the object referred to by
the pointer could be anywhere.
```

Mr Wojcik, my eyes are not what they used to be... the reference I supply 
states 'any data element (of a particular type)', not 'object'.  If there 
is another reference which states 'an object, and only an object, is 'any 
data element (of a particular type)'' then your point is valid... if not 
then other conclusions might not only be possible, but valid.

As I stated earlier... things might have changed in the decades since I 
was taught about this.

DD

I should have added that in the late 60's the "Journal of the ACM" had an 
article that said something to the effect that all processing could be 
driven by thorough description of data structures.  That's why I like COBOL 
so much.  By describing data structures, the code needed to deal with 
arithmetic or logic can ignore these details.  The programmer is unlikely to 
scribble on memory outside of his data division.  No wonder more lines of 
COBOL code (a quarter of a trillion lines?) are in use today, with billions 
more written every year.  And consider the number of lines of lower level 
language that are needed to do what single lines of COBOL do.  "string" and 
"unstring delimited by" are good examples.  I dread the flaming responses 
that these comments will cause.  Please, just let me express my opinion 
without being punished.  Paul

Paul H wrote:
> I should have added that in the late 60's the "Journal of the ACM"
> had an article that said something to the effect that all processing
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> year.

Not intended as "punishment" (believe whatever you want to...) but:

1. The estimate of 5 billion lines of COBOL a year is suspect, but even if 
it were true, the lines written in Java alone are around 5 times that. (To 
be fair, these estimates are just as suspect as the COBOL ones... no-one 
REALLY knows.)

2. The quarter of a trillion lines of COBOL came from a Gartner report (as 
Gartner do business with a large COBOL user base it can hardly be seen as 
unbiased...) that is now over 10 years old. In that time billions of lines 
of that codebase have been eroded by conversion, replacement, and 
retirement, and this process proceeds and accellerates every year.

The point is that it doesn't matter how many LOC are written in ANY 
language; what matters is the cost of writing it and whether it can be or 
needs to be, maintained.

> And consider the number of lines of lower level language that
> are needed to do what single lines of COBOL do.  "string" and
> "unstring delimited by" are good examples.  I dread the flaming
> responses that these comments will cause.  Please, just let me
> express my opinion without being punished.  Paul

There are a number of powerful constructs in COBOL and these are just two of 
them. COBOL's problems were never with the power of the language.

This is not a "flaming" response, Paul, just civilized discussion and 
observation... :-)

Pete.
```

## Thread: OpenCOBOL 1.1 pre-release surpasses 9000 NIST standard tests — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Open-source COBOL (GnuCOBOL, OpenCOBOL, TinyCOBOL)`](topics.md#open-source) · [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards)_

### OpenCOBOL 1.1 pre-release surpasses 9000 NIST standard tests

- **From:** btiffin@canada.com
- **Date:** 2008-12-13T10:35:02-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a535198a-6fa0-4c81-aef9-de1a3e7e9718@e3g2000vbe.googlegroups.com>`

```
Recent builds of OpenCOBOL 1.1 now pass more than 9000 of the NIST
COBOL-85 tests.

Excuse me if the formatting gets bungled, but here is a recent
summary.txt after a make test pass with OpenCOBOL 1.1 dated after
December 8th, 2008.


------ Directory Information -------   --- Total Tests Information ---

SQ           81       81     0     0    512    0       6      81   599
RL           32       32     0     0   1827    0       5       0  1832
IX           39       39     0     0    507    0       1       0   508
ST           39       39     0     0    278    0       0       0   278

------ -------- -------- ----- -----  ----- ---- ------- ------- -----
Total       374      374     0     0   8964    0      25      93  9082

Brian Tiffin

<a id="t-05d2e4dbfd"></a>
## Thread: Anoterh test request (overlapping oerands again) — 17 msgs

### Anoterh test request (overlapping oerands again)

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-15T17:04:58-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<woB1l.347451$6p1.210393@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
The Standard ('85, '02, whatever) has rules for overlapping operand operations 
being undefined.  I have found (thanks to CLC people) that results DO differed 
for "SET" statements.  I am now interested in arithmetic statements.  I would 
appreciate CLC people testing the following ('85 Standard conforming) program 
with your "compiler of choice" (with compiler options/directives "of choice") 
and letting me know if all 5 tests display a "pass" message.

If all the test pass, just let me know which compiler you have tested with.  If 
any of them FAIL, I would be interested in the compiler and what results you 
get.
(I don't think there should be any "compile-time errors" - but if so, let me 
know that as well).

Test source code follows:

       Identification Division.
        Program-ID. OVERLAP2.
       Data Division.
        Working-Storage Section.
       01  Group1.
           05  Num1 Pic S9(02)
             Sign is Leading.
           05  Pic  X(01).
       01  Group2 redefines Group1.
           05  Num3 Pic S9(01)
            Sign is Leading.
           05  Num2     Pic S9(02)
              Sign is Trailing.
       Procedure Division.
        Mainline.
           Perform Add-Test
           Perform Subtract-Test
           Perform Multiply-Test
           Perform Divide-Test-1
           Perform Divide-Test-2
           Stop Run
             .
         Add-Test.
           Display " --> Start ADD test"
           Move -91  to Num2
           Move +23  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Add Num1 to Num2
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-ADD
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num2 = -8
                       Display "ADD Test passed, Num2=" Num2
                   Else
                       Display "ADD Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "ADD Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End ADD test"
            .
         Subtract-Test.
           Display " --> Start Subtract test"
           Move -91  to Num2
           Move +23  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Subtract Num1 from Num2
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-Subtract
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num2 = -54
                       Display "SUBTRACT Test passed, Num2=" Num2
                   Else
                       Display "SUBTRACT Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "SUBTRACT Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End Subtract test"
            .
         Multiply-Test.
           Display " --> Start Multiply test"
           Move -93  to Num2
           Move +2  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Multiply Num1 by Num2
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-Multiply
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num2 = -46
                       Display "MULTIPLY Test passed, Num2=" Num2
                   Else
                       Display "MULTIPLY Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "MULTIPLY Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End MULTIPLY test"
            .

         Divide-Test-1.
           Display " --> Start DIVIDE test 1"
           Move +99  to Num2
           Move -5  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Divide Num1 into Num2
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-DIVIDE
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num2 = -11
                       Display "DIVIDE Test passed, Num2=" Num2
                   Else
                       Display "DIVIDE Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "DIVIDE Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End DIVIDE test 1"
            .

         Divide-Test-2.
           Display " --> Start DIVIDE test 2"
           Move +99  to Num2
           Move -5  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Divide Num1 into Num2
                Giving Num2
                Remainder Num3
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-DIVIDE
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num2 = -11
                    And Num3 = +4
                       Display "DIVIDE Test passed, Num2=" Num2
                       DIsplay "                    Num3=" Num3
                   Else
                       Display "DIVIDE Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
                       Display "                    Num3=" Num3
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "DIVIDE Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End DIVIDE test 2"
            .

****** end of source code
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Anoterh test request (overlapping oerands again)

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-15T23:07:48-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<CIG1l.100099$5i4.7863@en-nntp-05.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<woB1l.347451$6p1.210393@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com> <49472318$0$5484$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
The "odd" results on VMS are not really "odd".  The Standard doesn't tell HOW a 
compiler is supposed to DISPLAY signed numeric fields.  IBM also does "funny" 
things with these displays.  They "combine" the number and the sign-nibble and 
display it as an alphabetic.

This is "conforming" and as the value shows as "passed" this is OK.
```

correctly, you need to move it to a numeric-edited field first - or (I think) 
use the SIGN IS SEPARATE clause.

## Thread: Still one more Overlapping operands test — 8 msgs

### Still one more Overlapping operands test

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-16T19:39:18-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bLY1l.352867$6p1.52192@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Following is another test program that I want to know if it passes on all 
systems or if/when it fails.  This program may NOT get a clean compile with all 
compilers.  The '02 Standard lets you do an IF NUMERIC test on PACKED=DECIMAL 
data items, but this was not allowed by the '85 Standard.  I think that many 
(possibly most) compilers DID allow it, but some tests might get compile time 
errors (or at least warnings).

After this, I may try one more test with binary data-items.

Please just let me know if this passes.  Only if it fails do I need/want to see 
the results.

Thanks to all in advance.

  ***

       Identification Division.
        Program-ID. OVERLAP3.
       Data Division.
        Working-Storage Section.
       01  Group1.
           05  Num1 Pic S9(05)
             Packed-Decimal.
           05  Num1-Dec redefines Num1  Pic S99V999
            Packed-Decimal.
           05  Pic  X(01).
       01  Group2 redefines Group1.
           05  Num3 Pic S9(01)
            Sign is Leading.
           05  Num3-Dec Redefines Num3  Pic SV9
            Sign is Leading.
           05  Num2     Pic S9(03)
              Packed-Decimal.
           05 Num2-Dec Redefines Num2  Pic SVpp9(03)
            Packed-Decimal.
           05    Pic X(01).
       01  Temp.
           05 NE        Pic +9(01).9(05).
       Procedure Division.
        Mainline.
           Perform Add-Test
           Perform Subtract-Test
           Perform Multiply-Test
           Perform Divide-Test-1
           Perform Divide-Test-2
           Stop Run
             .
         Add-Test.
           Display " --> Start ADD test"
           Move Zero  to Num2
           Move +123  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Add Num1 to Num2
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-ADD
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num2 = +246
                       Display "ADD Test passed, Num2=" Num2
                   Else
                       Display "ADD Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "ADD Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End ADD test"
            .
         Subtract-Test.
           Display " --> Start Subtract test"
           Move zero  to Num2
           Move -23456  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Subtract Num2 from Num1
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-Subtract
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num1 = -23000
                       Display "SUBTRACT Test passed, Num2=" Num1
                   Else
                       Display "SUBTRACT Test Failed, Num2=" Num1
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "SUBTRACT Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End Subtract test"
            .
         Multiply-Test.
           Display " --> Start Multiply test"
           Move zero  to Num2
           Move +21  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Multiply Num1 by Num2
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-Multiply
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num2 = +441
                       Display "MULTIPLY Test passed, Num2=" Num2
                   Else
                       Display "MULTIPLY Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "MULTIPLY Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End MULTIPLY test"
            .

         Divide-Test-1.
           Display " --> Start DIVIDE test 1"
           Move zero  to Num2
           Move -45678  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Divide Num2 into Num1-Dec
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-DIVIDE
               If Num1-Dec Numeric
                   If Num1-Dec = +0.067
                       Move Num1-Dec to NE
                       Display "DIVIDE Test passed, Num1=" NE
                   Else
                       Move Num1-Dec to NE
                       Display "DIVIDE Test Failed, Num1=" NE
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "DIVIDE Test Failed, Num1=" Group1
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End DIVIDE test 1"
            .
         Divide-Test-2.
           Display " --> Start DIVIDE test 2"
           Move zero  to Num2
           Move -98765  to Num1
           Evaluate
               Num1 Numeric
                 Also
               Num2 Numeric
             When False also False
               Display "Neither Numeric"
             When False also True
               Display "Num1 not numeric"
             When True also False
               Display "Num2 not numeric"
             When Other
               Divide Num1 into Num2
                Giving Num2-Dec
                Remainder Num3-Dec
                   On Size Error
                       Display "Size Error"
               End-DIVIDE
               If Num2 Numeric
                   If Num2-Dec = +0.00774
                    And Num3-Dec = -0.5
                       Move Num2-Dec to NE
                       Display "DIVIDE Test passed, Num2-Dec=" NE
                       Move Num3-Dec to NE
                       DIsplay "                    Num3-Dec=" NE
                   Else
                       Move Num2-Dec to NE
                       Display "DIVIDE Test Failed, Num2-N1=" NE
                       Move Num3-Dec to NE
                       Display "                    Num3-N2=" NE
                   End-If
               Else
                   Display "DIVIDE Test Failed, Num2=" Group2 (2:)
               End-IF
           End-Evaluate
           Display " -->   End DIVIDE test 2"
            .

* * *  End of sample source code

## Thread: some help with COBDATA please... — 3 msgs

### some help with COBDATA please...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-19T02:03:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6qv01qFemvfjU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
There has been a problem with forgotten or lost passwords being mailed to 
people who have registered on the cobdata web site.

I believe this is now fixed (I amended code and configurations, and the tech 
support people claim that the site is now fine, although there were some 
network problems intermittently for a while).

The kind of sad thng is that I can test this from my system and it works 
fine, yet I am not totally convinced it REALLY works. This is because my 
system is running IIS and the cobdata site COULD be using IIS to do the 
mailing, with it all happening so fast, I am not aware of it. (In other 
words it MIGHT only work if I am online, and if I go offline I can't test 
it... :-) (yeah, I know... this web programming stuff is crazy)

I COULD stop IIS and run some more tests but there are some implications in 
doing this which make it an unattractive option.

What I really need is for someone who has previously registered on the 
cobdata web site and is totally disconnected from me and my network, to try 
retrieving their password.

Could somebody do this please?

Go to http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobdata and in the logon boxes enter your 
previously registered email address and a ? in the password box. Click logon 
and see if it mails you your password.

Clicking "Resume" should bring you back to the home page.

Obviously, I only need one such person so it would be appreciated if you 
could post here once you have done it. (Maybe we should have a no-prize for 
being first... :-))

It is my intention to post the full source code of both the COBOL structure 
tool, and the "String2Num" ActiveX/COM component, on the site, this weekend.

The site has currently taken around 1100 hits and the structure tool has 
been successfully downloaded 34 times.

Thanks,

Pete.
```

<a id="t-4acc5d7b82"></a>
## Thread: Final (I hope) Overlapping Artihmetic test — 10 msgs

### Final (I hope) Overlapping Artihmetic test

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-19T02:23:15-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<URI2l.457520$5p1.197206@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Once again, could kindly CLC readers try the following source code on various 
compilers and let me know what the restuls are.

Testing portability of "BINARY" stuff is almost impossible.  Not only do 
"standard" vs "normal NON-COBOL" truncation rules come into play, but 
big-/little-endian issues and word/byte boundaries and synchronozation also 
impacts how compilers do this.

THEREFORE, it won't surprise me if many compilers "fail" this test.  I but in a 
"check" for big-/little-endian, but couldn't try all the variations of sync and 
binary storatge issues.

This may not even compile on all compilers as I have used "hex-literals" and 
some other stuff that isn't standard.

If anyone has any suggestions on how to test this in a "more portable" way (for 
BINARY data items), please let me know.

Please, just let me know the results.

  * * * * * * * *

       Identification Division.
        Program-ID. OVERLAP4.
       Data Division.
        Working-Storage Section.
       01  Check-Format.
           05  Test-Bin  Pic S9(09) Binary  Value +12345678.
       01  Group1.
           05  Num1  Pic S9(09) Binary.
           05   Pic  X(04).
       01  Group2 Redefines Group1.
           05   Pic  X(02).
           05  Num2  Pic S9(09) Binary.
       01  Group3 Redefines Group1.
           05                   Pic  X(04).
           05  Num3  Pic S9(04) Binary.
           05   Pic  X(02).
       01  Group4 Redefines Group1.
           05  Num4             Pic S99999V99 Binary.
           05  Num5             Pic S99V999 Binary.
       Procedure Division.
        Mainline.
           Evaluate Check-Format
             When X"00BC614E"
              Perform Big-Endian
             When X"4E61BC00"
                Perform Little-Endian
             When Other
                Display "Unexpected Binary Format, no tests Performed"
           End-Evaluate
    Stop Run
     .
       Big-Endian.
           Display "==> Start Big-Endian Tests"
           Move Zeroes   to Num2
           Move +10101010  To Num1
           Add Num1 to Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> ADD had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-ADD
           If Num2 = +564928786
               Display "      ADD Test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display "      ADD Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
           Move Zeroes   to Num3
           Move +1234567 To Num2
           Subtract Num3 From Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> SUBTRACT had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-SUBTRACT
           If Num2 = +1245184
               Display " SUBTRACT Test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display " SUBTRACT Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
           Move Zeroes  to Num2
           Move +123  to Num3
           Multiply Num3 By Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> MULTIPLY had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-MULTIPLY
           If Num2 = +15129
               Display " MULTIPLY test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display " MULTIPLY test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
           Move Zeroes  to Num1
                                   Num3
           Move +7286841 to Num2
           Divide Num2 by Num3
             Giving Num4
             Remainder Num5
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> DIVIDE had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-Divide
           If   Num4 = +590.26
            and Num5 = +81.3
               Display "   DIVIDE test Passed, Num4=" Num4
               DISPLAY "                       Num5=" Num5
           Else
               Display "   DIVIDE test Failed, Num4=" Num4
               DISPLAY "                       Num5=" Num5
           End-IF
           Display "==>   End Big-Endian Tests"
            .
       Little-Endian.
           Display "==> Start Little-Endian Tests"
           Move Zeroes   to Num2
           Move +10101010  To Num1
           Add Num1 to Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> ADD had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-ADD
           If Num2 = +10101164
               Display "      ADD Test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display "      ADD Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
           Move Zeroes   to Num3
           Move +1234567 To Num2
           Subtract Num3 From Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> SUBTRACT had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-SUBTRACT
           If Num2 = +1234549
               Display " SUBTRACT Test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display " SUBTRACT Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
           Move Zeroes  to Num2
           Move +4915200 to Num2
           Multiply Num3 By Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> MULTIPLY had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-MULTIPLY
           If Num2 = +368640000
               Display " MULTIPLY test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display " MULTIPLY test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
           Move Zeroes  to Num1
                                   Num3
           Move +864224 to Num2
           Divide Num2 by Num3
             Giving Num4
             Remainder Num5
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> DIVIDE had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-Divide
           If   Num4 = +66478.76
            and Num5 = +0.120
               Display "   DIVIDE test Passed, Num4=" Num4
               DISPLAY "                       Num5=" Num5
           Else
               Display "   DIVIDE test Failed, Num4=" Num4
               DISPLAY "                       Num5=" Num5
           End-IF
           Display "==>   End Little-Endian Tests"
            .             .

Looks like HP3K Cobol doesn't support hex-literals (not part of the '85 
Standard).

I don't fully understand the  PowerPC based iSeries machine problem, but I think 
it has to do with how many bytes are allocated for which size binary field. 
Which compiler were you using on that machine? I see "5.4" but don't know what 
compiler that is.  (Is that ILE COBOL or COBOL/400 or something else?)
```
