I have come up with a variation of my original test code that should be MORE 
portable.  I think it will compile on HP, but will probably still have a problem 
on iSeries.

If anyone is interested in testing and hasn't already, please use this code 
instead of the original code:

       Identification Division.
        Program-ID. OVERLAP5.
       Data Division.
        Working-Storage Section.
       01  Group1.
           05  Num1  Pic S9(09) Binary.
           05   Pic  X(04).
       01  Group2 Redefines Group1.
           05   Pic  X(02).
           05  Num2  Pic S9(09) Binary.
       01  Group3 Redefines Group1.
           05                   Pic  X(04).
           05  Num3  Pic S9(04) Binary.
           05   Pic  X(02).
       01  Group4 Redefines Group1.
           05  Num4             Pic S99999V99 Binary.
           05  Num5             Pic S99V999 Binary.
       01  Sep.
           05  Num1-Sep  Pic S9(09) Binary Sync.
           05  Num2-Sep  Pic S9(09) Binary Sync.
           05  Num3-Sep  Pic S9(04) Binary Sync.
           05  Num4-Sep  Pic S99999V99 Binary Sync.
           05  Num5-Sep  Pic S99V999 Binary Sync.
       Procedure Division.
        Mainline.
           Perform Add-test
           Perform Subtract-test
           Perform Multiply-Test
           Perform Divide-Test
          Stop Run
            .
       ADD-Test.
           Move Zeroes   to Num2
           Move +10101010  To Num1
           Move Num2  to Num2-Sep
           Add Num1 to Num2-Sep
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> ADD had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-ADD
           Move Zeroes   to Num2
           Move +10101010  To Num1
           Add Num1 to Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> ADD had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-ADD
           If Num2 = Num2-Sep
               Display "      ADD Test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display "      ADD Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
            .
       Subtract-Test.
           Move Zeroes   to Num3
           Move +1234567 To Num2
           Move Num2  to Num2-Sep
           Subtract Num3 From Num2-Sep
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> SUBTRACT had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-SUBTRACT
           Move Zeroes   to Num3
           Move +1234567 To Num2
           Subtract Num3 From Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> SUBTRACT had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-SUBTRACT
           If Num2 = Num2-Sep
               Display " SUBTRACT Test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display " SUBTRACT Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
            .
       Multiply-Test.
           Move Zeroes  to Num2
           Move +123  to Num3
           Move Num2  to Num2-Sep
           Multiply Num3 By Num2-Sep
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> MULTIPLY had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-MULTIPLY
           Move Zeroes  to Num2
           Move +123  to Num3
           Multiply Num3 By Num2
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> MULTIPLY had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-MULTIPLY
           If Num2 = Num2-Sep
               Display " MULTIPLY Test Passed, Num2=" Num2
           Else
               Display " MULTIPLY Test Failed, Num2=" Num2
           End-If
            .
       Divide-Test.
           Move Zeroes  to Num1
                                   Num3
           Move +7286841 to Num2
           Move Num4  to Num4-Sep
           Move Num5  to Num5-Sep
           Divide Num2 by Num3
             Giving Num4-Sep
             Remainder Num5-Sep
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> DIVIDE had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-Divide
           Move Zeroes  to Num1

           Divide Num2 by Num3
             Giving Num4
             Remainder Num5
               On Size Error
                   Display "-> DIVIDE had an ON SIZE ERROR"
           End-Divide
           If   Num4 = Num4-Sep
            and Num5 = Num5-Sep
               Display "   DIVIDE Test Passed, Num4=" Num4
               DISPLAY "                       Num5=" Num5
           Else
               Display "   DIVIDE Test Failed, Num4=" Num4

 William M. Klein wrote:
> > Once again, could kindly CLC readers try the following source code on
various
> > compilers and let me know what the restuls are.
> >
> > Testing portability of "BINARY" stuff is almost impossible.  Not only do
> > "standard" vs "normal NON-COBOL" truncation rules come into play, but
> > big-/little-endian issues and word/byte boundaries and synchronozation
also
> > impacts how compilers do this.
> >
> > THEREFORE, it won't surprise me if many compilers "fail" this test.  I
but in a
> > "check" for big-/little-endian, but couldn't try all the variations of
sync and
> > binary storatge issues.
> >
> > This may not even compile on all compilers as I have used "hex-literals"
and
> > some other stuff that isn't standard.
> >
> > If anyone has any suggestions on how to test this in a "more portable"
way (for
> > BINARY data items), please let me know.
> >
> > Please, just let me know the results.
>

## Thread: Can Open COBOL do this...? — 11 msgs

### Can Open COBOL do this...?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-21T20:29:24+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6r69ioFfssi0U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Can anybody confirm or deny whether Open COBOL supports the following two 
things, please? :

1. OO constructs. Is it possible to write Classes and objects using OO COBOL 
language elements, with Open COBOL?

Bill, can you confirm whether OO COBOL is part of the 2002 standard, please?

2. Component Object Model (COM) wrappings for .DLL registration and 
interfacing?

If it doesn't is anything in these areas planned?

I could see this being an ideal tool for moving Current COBOL apps onto the 
Network, with minimal expense.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Can Open COBOL do this...?

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-21T04:54:55-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4gp3l.43774$Rp1.19545@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<6r69ioFfssi0U1@mid.individual.net> <6b4744d7-958b-4214-aa34-5dc2e1e9ec35@l39g2000yqn.googlegroups.com>`

```
Yes, I can confirm that OO is part of the '02 Standard.  (There is NOTHING that 
is called or is a "97" Standard.

In the '02 Standard all of Object Orientation (as defined there) is required. 
There is possibility that some of this (particularly "factory objects") will be 
made optional in the nest Standard.

OpenCOBOL started with implementing the '85 Standard and many of the most common 
extensions to that.  It is gradually adding features from the '02 and draft 
revision. (While still "cleaning up" features of the '85 Standard and 
amendments, e.g. the ALL subscript for functions and various aspects of nested 
programming.)
```

It is a bit beyond linkage to .DLLs, Brian. It needs to have certain 
interfaces implemented (like IDispatch) and it needs to have marshalling and 
support for type libraries and .DLL registration... there's a fair bit to 
it. Probably most of it already exists in MS C++ libraries (I know C++ 
supports COM) but I don't know enough about that environment to be sure. 
Adding this functionality means COBOL can be used to build components that 
can run anywhere. Both MicroFocus and Fujitsu support it.

to the same DB 'file'. I can see what you did, and it is quite 
justified, but there are other alternatives, which I will illustrate.
 From my viewpoint I think you arrived at a solution, using Factory, and 
got 'locked-in' seeing that as the way to your intended result.

I'd be interested in knowing who in the current Standard's 'clan' is 
promoting the idea of removing Factory. It does have its uses and with 
Micro Focus you can specify :-

		MyClass is ABSTRACT
		inherits from .....

Where specifically annotated 'Abstract' you only have Factory methods 
and you cannot create Instances - I tried out of curiosity and got an 
RTS Error 241 - instances cannot be created for Abstract classes.

I've more or less finished on my coding for OO Factory so might possibly

…[34 more quoted lines elided]…

Perhaps Brian is thinking along Procedural lines, Local Storage 
sections, (also allowable within Procedural), holding functions.

Pete - cast your mind back to SoftwareSimple - At one stage I suggested 
(diplomatically), that the then J4 could arrive at a common set of 
Collection/Dictionary classes, (and with no info on Fujitsu - there were 
no on-line manuals at the time), both F/J and M/F could adapt their 
classes, using PRIVATE methods to feed into the common standard. When it 
comes to support utilities/classes, as you've indicated for C# - 4,000 
or was that 40,000, the M/F Collections/Dictionaries are a FRACTION of 
the M/F support classes.

With a push from Bill, J4 eventually started to get into 
Collections/Dictionaries. So far as I am concerned the thinking was 
based ENTIRELY on the Fujitsu approach - God know where they got there 
concept from, with no ADDITIONAL support classes. As you are no doubt 
aware from my previous messages, Micro Focus took Smalltalk as their 
model; I can't be certain but I think Hitachi may have done the same.
(The latter were certainly provided with a seminar on OO concepts, 
before they got cracking).

I was questioned, "Do we really need Dictionaries ?". Call them 
Dictionaries or Maps, - the answer is "YES".

Then J4 in their wisdom rejected my suggestion that Collections SHOULD 
allow for individual elements to be CHANGED. (Example - you produce a 
Listbox to go into a Dialog containing a SortedCollection. This gent's 
name is written "William M. Kleen". According to the J4 thinking - no 
can do - BUT - you can DELETE the element for Mr. Kleen and substitute 
an ADD element for "William M. Klein".  Real neat ! ).

The kindest thing J4/PL22.4 could do is leave OO COBOL well alone.  I 
can't think there is any 'push' from IBM to change; they rely on Java to 
support the basic COBOL class format, and it is Java which creates and 
controls their COBOL objects. Fujitsu and Micro Focus - why would they 
change - they are both rolling down the road with dotNet, saves them the 
bother of OO COBOL enhancements.

As regards OpenCOBOL - frankly they would be absolutely wasting their 
time UNLESS they come up with a full bodied and rich set of utility 
classes - and they just don't have the resources. Even taking existing 
models, my best guess, (as they are volunteers) - some two years to 
think out the total support utility set for COBOL and then - another one 
or two years to code and implement. Again, makes it too long after the 
event - folks will be using dotNet.

Jimmy, Calgary AB

I guess it is arguable. If you have OO support you can certainly write your 
own Collection classes. Everything comes down to priorities, really. I'd 
rather see things like Regular Expressions in COBOL than Collections, 
personally, but everyone has their own view and none of them are wrong.


>
> Then J4 in their wisdom rejected my suggestion that Collections SHOULD
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> an ADD element for "William M. Klein".  Real neat ! ).

I understand your frustration. But you are talking about an approach that is 
just years out of date. These days you would simply bind your list box to 
your data source and change "Kleen" on the data source. It would 
automatically be propagated instantly to the list box by a 'refresh' method. 
The fact is that modern software will AUTOMATICALLY create the "collection" 
for you transparently (whether it is a dynaset or a result set from an SQL 
query, or something else.) There are times when you want to throw something 
into a collection and you may want it ordered or hashed, but these times are 
not that frequent and there are constructs in other languages that make it 
very easy to enumerate and process "collections" whether they are officially 
Collections or not.  I can use "foreach" in C# to treat a sequential file 
exactly as if it were a collection, for example.

I know you like code so here's a sample...

I have created a collection of  metadata lines (called TP6MOSTMTD) 
describing a COBOL record layout.. This is really a series of element 
descriptions and was created from a sequential file by reading the file and 
shoving each line into the collection. Each line has information about the 
dataname, level number, record it bolngs to, type, group or element, occurs, 
redefines, offset in the record, and length. (This last was derived by 
runnning newcobdata as a component, from C#. I originally had my own code 
doing it, but I liked Robert's better, so I simply embedded the component 
into my C# application...That's the point about component based 
applications; you can easily replace a component, with no knock on effects, 
because everything is encapsulated)

Now I want another collection that is just the datanames, enclosed in square 
brackets, and I want to ensure that the first entry is the primary key field 
(or fields), so, as I get the datanames I prefix them with a "0" for primary 
key, and a "1" for everything else. This list of datanames will be used to 
generate lists for SQL statements.

The code iterates over the metadata (MTD) collection and creates a String 
Array (which is really another kind of collection).

// build a collection of COBOL datanames from the MTD
string subsetline = "";
ArrayList subset = new ArrayList();
subset.Clear();
foreach (string metaline in TP6MOSTMTD)
{
    if (metaline.Substring(79, 1) == "1") // prime key
    {
        subsetline = "0[" + metaline.Substring(89, 35).Trim() + "]";
        subset.Add(subsetline);
    }
    else
    {
        if (metaline.Substring(0, 35) == RGTableName.PadRight(35))
        {
            subsetline = "1[" + metaline.Substring(89, 35).Trim() + "]";
            subset.Add(subsetline);
        }
    }
}
subset.Sort(); // should get prime key first
// do the fields...
foreach (string COBOLname in subset)
{
    if (COBOLname.Substring(0, 1) == "0")
    {

        line = "                    " + "(" + COBOLname.Substring(1, 
COBOLname.Length - 1) + ",";
        line = line.PadRight(120);
        sw = TP6writeCOBSource(sw, "WRITE", line);
    }
    else
    {
        line = "                    " + COBOLname.Substring(1, 
COBOLname.Length - 1) + ",";
        line = line.PadRight(120);
        sw = TP6writeCOBSource(sw, "WRITE", line);
    }
}

While this is not particularly elegant code, it does demonstrate how 
"natural" the processing of collections is in C#. Notice that collections 
have innate methods like "Sort" and "Clear" which does make life pretty 
easy. You can also retrieve individual items with an index or value, as you 
would expect.

In procedural COBOL I'm not sure how valuable the provision of Collection 
functionality might be (or how much use would be made of it...). It is 
certainly desirable for OO, because the whole collection can be considered 
as a single object with its own methods and properties...
>
> The kindest thing J4/PL22.4 could do is leave OO COBOL well alone.  I
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> them the bother of OO COBOL enhancements.

I think they see their .NET compilers as an easy migration for procedural 
COBOL to the network. Just recompile your existing app. into CLR and away 
you go... This approach actually buys you the worst of two worlds. It is 
like running in emulation or compatibility mode. The Network needs objects 
and to get the best from it you need to provide objects to it. Running large 
monolithic COBOL apps on it is NOT a good idea... I strongly recommend 
people thinking of migrating, to refactor their COBOL code into components 
before they do so. I'll be talking more about this on the new cobdata site 
when it gets released (probably after Xmas now as I want a break...)
>
> As regards OpenCOBOL - frankly they would be absolutely wasting their
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> after the event - folks will be using dotNet.

Using .NET doesn't preclude the use of COBOL. (But it needs to be OO COBOL, 
ideally wrapped as COM) I can see OpenCOBOL as an ideal way to bring 
existing procedural code off the mainframe and into the Network, PROVIDED 
OpenCOBOL can support OO and COM wrapping. (Hence my recent question here.) 
If I was a C++ whiz, I'd gladly contribute to this project, but I'm not and 
I just don't have time to acquire that skill at the moment. I see OO support 
and COM wrapping as a much higher priority for OpenCOBOL than Collections or 
even utilities, because this would make the product commercially viable, 
but, as mentioned earlier, it is arguable. It is really a question of 
whether OpenCOBOL will support use of COBOL in the 21st century or remain 
tied to COBOL's 1985 roots.

Enjoy your Xmas!

Pete.
```

```
Hello,

I missed the part about "why User FUNCTION influencing Object COBOL in
OpenCOBOL" bit when I was duckin' and coverin' for spreading the 97
myth. ;)

I could well be speaking out of turn, but this is my take on the
potential for Object COBOL in future OpenCOBOL releases:

Currently the Intrinsic FUNCTION and other compiler internals for
prototyping fields is in C code sections deemed BEWARE; DO NOT TOUCH.
For implementation of User FUNCTION this prototyping code will need to
be exposed to higher levels of the code generation sequence.  That
exposure and lifting of the code from internal to developer accessible
would be a first step toward Object COBOL.  As far as I know; which
really isn't a whole lot.

Re; .NET.  Jimmy, I'm not sure that I agree that in a few years
everyone will be .NET.  Doesn't float my boat.  Forth, REBOL, COBOL,
good ol' C, Java, (I'll stop listing now...) will be in commercial
projects for the foreseeable future.  Open is gaining, and from my
point of view, gaining at the expense of closed-proprietary, not by
expanding into new territory.

I look forward to the next few years/decades and one (somewhat
unrelated) prediction I will make is that the Grid will smack a lot of
people upside the head ... when it is too late for them to do anything
but react.  Remember how MS ignored the WWW at first, then scrambled
so hard and fast that the DoJ took notice? (simplified view of history
there and I'm only using it as an example, not "truth").  It looks to
me that many professionals are currently doing the same with Grid
computing and the inevitable (IMHO) paradigm shift that is now upon
us.

Cheers,
Brian

I am NOT (usually) a "Wikipedia" person.  However, I did update the history

It's a cloudless Monday morning here in Tauranga and we are heading into the 
home straight for Xmas Day.

Today I'll be fiddling with the cobdata web site, picking up a turkey, and 
taking a walk along a less populated beach. A time for pleasant relaxation.

"absent friends".

That includes you guys... :-)

It is just one day of the year when I think about the people who are

religious occasion for you, I hope you find peace and serenity. If it isn't,

<a id="t-93ffac730d"></a>
## Thread: On User Defined Functions and OO — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Object-oriented COBOL`](topics.md#oo)_

### On User Defined Functions and OO

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-22T22:26:30-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<WLZ3l.3469$kG.2563@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
I am not now, nor was I ever a "compiler developer" (or even a run-time 
developer).  I know COBOL for its "externals" (and I know those VERY well).

However,
  in response to the "what does User defined functions have to do with adding OO 
to OpenCOBOL" question, I did some thinking.  I may be totally wrong on this, 
but here is my take on this.  (Probably NOT what was meant by the original 
poster).

To me, there are "two flavors" (or externals) for Object Orientation (at least 
in COBOL).  The source code that "uses" options via
  - INVOKE statement
  - inline object reference

Such code may be "traditional procedural COBOL code" *or* it may be within the 
methods of an Object itself.

From an explicitly EXTERNAL point of view, the compiler developer really needs 
to do very little more for INVOKE than CALL (especially CALL with returning) 
already supports.  Furthermore, again from my external point of view,
  CALL with RETURNING is to User (or intrinsic) Functions
      as
  INVOKE is to inline object references

I can (relatively) easily imagine both of these being coded by compiler 
developers in "similar" ways.

Now, when it comes to RUN-TIME support for these,  there is a lot more than 
"traditional" CALL support.  There is "resolution" of what method is being 
invoked as determined by the object being "used".  Furthermore, there is the 
whole question of "conformance" of parameters (especially with polymorphism and 
method overloading).  I may be crazy on this, but I do see these as run-time, 
not compiler issues.  Furthermore, for those implementations that include 
support for the (extension) ENTRY feature of COBOL, some of the "resolution" 
issues may already be solved (at least in part).  And with "parameter 
conformance" and "call prototypes" in the 2002 Standard, I can imagine the OO 
features being solved in "similar" (NOT identical) ways as features that are 
required by non-OO support.

Now, as far as compiler (and run-time) support for CLASS definitions and the 
entire "OO structure" of source code, this does require a different set of 
compiler support.  But again, with nested programs, global and local data, even 
EXTERNAL data that "persists" across programs, I would think that much (not all) 
of the "development" may be there.  And once again, support for multiple ENTRY 
statements within a "procedural" program MAY be the starting point for 
developing compile and run-time support for multiple methods within the same 
class definition.

And back to user defined functions, it would seem to me that if a compiler did 
support these (and their parameter conformance checking issues and "inline" 
syntax) much of this could be used as the basis for OO support in the compiler 
(and possibly even the run-time).

  ***

What needs to be made SUPER clear with this note is that I am *not* saying that 
the "paradigm" of coding OO COBOL is that similar to existing (traditional 
procedural) COBOL.  I am only saying that I can imagine compiler and run-time 
support for nested programming, ENTRY (extension), and user defined functions as 
being the "implementer" starting point for providing OO support in a COBOL 
implementation.

However, as I started out, it is also important to say that I am not an 
implementor, so what would seem "similar" to me for externals may well actually 
provide little or no help to developing a full OO COBOL compiler (and run-time).

As usual, your mileage may vary <G>
```

#### ↳ Re: On User Defined Functions and OO

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-24T09:16:47+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6rcv9hFmlr9U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<WLZ3l.3469$kG.2563@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> I am not now, nor was I ever a "compiler developer" (or even a
> run-time developer).  I know COBOL for its "externals" (and I know
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> compiler developers in "similar" ways.

The mechanics are certainly similar.
>
> Now, when it comes to RUN-TIME support for these,  there is a lot
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> issues.

That depends on whether the object is early or late bound. I only use late 
bound, so for me at least, it is a runtime issue, but with early bound code 
compliance is checked at compile time, using the Repository to obtain 
formats and prototypes.


 Furthermore, for those implementations that include support
> for the (extension) ENTRY feature of COBOL, some of the "resolution"
> issues may already be solved (at least in part).

Hmmm... I think I understand what you're saying, Bill, but not sure about 
that. Reference to entry points can be resolved statically or dynamically, 
as you know, and the mechanism is different for each of these. Nevertheless, 
there are general similarities and if a compiler writer already had this 
covered, that code could almost certainly serve as a template for method 
invocation.


>  And with "parameter
> conformance" and "call prototypes" in the 2002 Standard, I can
> imagine the OO features being solved in "similar" (NOT identical)
> ways as features that are required by non-OO support.

Yes, that sems likely.
>
> Now, as far as compiler (and run-time) support for CLASS definitions
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> the same class definition.

It's a bit tenuous I think, but in principle, "probably" :-)
>
> And back to user defined functions, it would seem to me that if a
> compiler did support these (and their parameter conformance checking
> issues and "inline" syntax) much of this could be used as the basis
> for OO support in the compiler (and possibly even the run-time).

You'd think so...
>
>  ***
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> starting point for providing OO support in a COBOL implementation.

I agree that would make sense if a compiler was being built from scratch. 
The difficulty arises, I think, in having to "bolt on" the OO functionality 
without upsetting what is already in place. I have never ceased to be amazed 
by the engineers who did this for Fujitsu and Micro Focus; it is an 
extraordinary piece of work.

>
> However, as I started out, it is also important to say that I am not
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> As usual, your mileage may vary <G>

I take your points that there is mechanical similarity in some of the 
existing functionality, but neither of us are actually going to do it and it 
really comes down to what the implementor already has to work with. I think 
if I were tasked todo this, I would use existing code as a model but would 
keep the whole area separate from the rest of the compiler, even if this 
meant some duplication of code.

It would certainly be an interesting challenge... :-)

Pete.
```

## Thread: NEWS for Fujtissu COBOL users — 12 this month / 27 total

_Thread started [2008-11](2008-11.md#t-830abc0151)._

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: NEWS for Fujitsu COBOL users

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** yyyc186 <yyyc186@hughes.net>
- **Date:** 2008-12-27T18:03:56-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e9fb9226-6767-4376-a5bb-e557bdf62c2c@x8g2000yqk.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<xvmTk.163083$Fs5.45452@fe10.news.easynews.com> <6o6gk5F240hkU1@mid.individual.net> <c31bf062-8ce8-476c-8b6c-25b02fb0c456@s9g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <6o912hF2d3f6U1@mid.individual.net> <73522e56-33b6-4992-8cdf-0a4c7eb45462@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <6ofqvfF39gbeU1@mid.individual.net>`

…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Ah.  So your "methodology" is really nothing more than HACKING.  Just
keep throwing out broken-busted-short-sighted code until _something_
either gets accepted by the user or they cancel the project.

When you are talking about back-end systems, ITERATION = FAILURE.
There is no excuse for it.

User interfaces are never correct because no 2 users can agree on what
they want.  Limited iteration happens there while the user sees and
changes their mind.

Years later, when they want to add something to the system, is where
OO hands out failures of biblical proportions.  Adding a step or a
data set in the middle of the process because of some new government
regulation spells catastrophe for systems developed with OO that tried
to achieve the Utopian Inheritance Path.  They end up being almost a
complete re-write.  3GL systems simply end up with a wart in the
middle.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: NEWS for Fujitsu COBOL users

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-29T11:04:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6rqbg7F2s2ddU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<xvmTk.163083$Fs5.45452@fe10.news.easynews.com> <6o6gk5F240hkU1@mid.individual.net> <c31bf062-8ce8-476c-8b6c-25b02fb0c456@s9g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <6o912hF2d3f6U1@mid.individual.net> <73522e56-33b6-4992-8cdf-0a4c7eb45462@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <6ofqvfF39gbeU1@mid.individual.net> <e9fb9226-6767-4376-a5bb-e557bdf62c2c@x8g2000yqk.googlegroups.com>`

```
yyyc186 wrote:
> On Nov 18, 6:32 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> Ah.  So your "methodology" is really nothing more than HACKING.

I'm not sure what your definition of HACKING (your emphasis) is, but how 
would "proper object modelling" and runthroughs of paper models equate to 
that?

>Just
> keep throwing out broken-busted-short-sighted code until _something_
> either gets accepted by the user or they cancel the project.

No, that hasn't been my experience.

>
> When you are talking about back-end systems, ITERATION = FAILURE.
> There is no excuse for it.

NO iteration means that everything is defined and designed from a fixed set 
of requirements, despite the fact that these requirements are changing even 
as the software is being built. This is the classic flaw in the Waterfall 
approach; you are delivering something that is "something like" what was 
required 6 months ago when the Requirements Gathering took place.

Back-end systems are certainly less volatile than front-end ones but that 
doesn't mean you don't iterate development. I also mentioned (although you 
seem to have ignored it in your response) "interaction". That means joint 
development, with users involved throughout the process. It is therefore 
impossible NOT to iterate the requirements in such a joint development 
environment. It comes down to users deciding priorities when extensive 
change is required. I have found they do this well, provided they are kept 
in the loop. Instead of IT thrusting systems on users, they are an integral 
part of the development process and feel that the system is "theirs".

You said that your company uses offshore workers, so it is unlikely that 
joint development is being undertaken.

That is not a reflection on MY methodology, neither does it mean that what I 
do is hacking.
>
> User interfaces are never correct because no 2 users can agree on what
> they want.  Limited iteration happens there while the user sees and
> changes their mind.

What a warped and cynical view. If a system is being used by several 
different departments you make sure that all of them sit around a table and 
the requirements from each of them are considered and examined. You find 
points of agreement and make compromises. If there are really divergent 
requirements you provide two different interfaces.

IT is a service; it is there to HELP people, not to impose solutions that 
are technically convenient on them. You may use prototyping to help them 
visualise the end product, but you don't build ANYTHING until everyone is 
satisfied. And, as a Project Manager, you foster interaction between users 
and look for areas where functionality can usefully be shared. Very often 
they will come up with ideas that the systems analysts missed. Can't get 2 
users to agree on a requirement? I've come to EXPECT to have an average of 6 
departments agreeing, and have no problem with it. (in fact, on one 
occasion, I had a project that involved 14 user departments. It was for 
British Telecom, was jointly developed and was a big success. The users made 
fantastic contributions to it.)

If you were a bit less cynical and a bit more accommodating you might find 
users easier to deal with.

Some of us see giving people what they want and need as a satisfying career. 
Helping them to determine those requirements is part of it, but should never 
be allowed to drive it. When that happens you simply have IT imposing their 
own hidden agendas on the business, as if the business were "the enemy".... 
When the requirements are difficult that is a chance to exercise our skill 
and imagination and achieve job satisfaction because we did something 
extraordinary. Balancing all of the varying requirements into a coherent 
technical architecture is why they need us. The tail does NOT wag the dog...

>
> Years later, when they want to add something to the system, is where
> OO hands out failures of biblical proportions.

Really?  I won't bother to point out the logical fallacy in your sweeping 
statement, I'll just revert to reading about disasters in the Bible...

>Adding a step or a
> data set in the middle of the process because of some new government
> regulation spells catastrophe for systems developed with OO that tried
> to achieve the Utopian Inheritance Path.

I would agree that can certainly happen if proper modelling is not 
undertaken.

It is hardly fair to decide that OOP is useless and OO approaches are a 
recipe for disaster, based on your experience in a shop where, apparently, 
none of the things which would ensure success are in place. Many companies 
all over the world are using OO and component based approaches very 
successfully with shorter build times, easier maintenance and very happy 
users who feel like they own the systems.

>They end up being almost a
> complete re-write.  3GL systems simply end up with a wart in the
> middle.

Not much fun in your world, is there...?

Pete.

In article <6rqbg7F2s2ddU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>yyyc186 wrote:

[snip]

Prefatory note - please, be so kind as to read this in its entirety before 
responding... parts may seem harsh but the overall view might be a bit 
different.

>> User interfaces are never correct because no 2 users can agree on what
>> they want.  Limited iteration happens there while the user sees and
>> changes their mind.
>
>What a warped and cynical view.

What a facile denigration of what might be decades of experience in dozens 
of shops.  Mr Dashwood, just as you've constantly reminded me that 'things 
might, possibly, be different in other places than in the ones in which 
you've done work' so, too, might be the case for you.

>If a system is being used by several 
>different departments you make sure that all of them sit around a table and 
>the requirements from each of them are considered and examined. You find 
>points of agreement and make compromises.

That is job for Management, Mr Dashwood, not for someone whose job it is 
to sling code... and even then it might not be as easy as 'all ya gotta do 
is get People of Good Intentions in a group and say 'Come, let us reason 
together'.

I was told a tale, decades back, of an interface that was being designed 
for multiple groups.  After a bunch of 'I want... no, *I* want... no, *we* 
absolutely have to have' memoranda went back and forth the IT Manager for 
the project arranged A Meeting between the seniormost of the parties 
concerned in order to smooth things out.

In a large conference-room he set up a terminal and a projector 
(state-of-the-art, back then) and put his very best code-jockey at the 
keyboard.  He then opened the floor for discussion... what is the 
entry-screen into this system going to look like?

The system was a WANG VS, one which had excellent utilities for 
generating screens and reports... literally, 'all ya hadda do' was put the 
text and entry-variables onto the screen and a utility would generate 
code.  Easy as pie, right?

Wrong.  They started with the heading, usually something like:

          ENGULF & DEVOUR, INC.
   INTERACTIVE PHLOGISTON MANAGEMENT SYSTEM
(date) PRIMARY SYSTEM OPERATIONS MENU (time)

... and the disagreements began to fly.  One group demanded a line stating 
that this was for Inventory Control, another for Customer Account 
Management, someone wanted MM/DD/YY format, another DD/MM/YY, a third 
wanted YYYYMMDD... someone said the date should be in 12-hr format with 
AM/PM suffix while another wanted a 24-hour clock...

... and the IT Director had the code-jockey go through these, one at a 
time... insert a line here, change a format, delete a line there, go back, 
do it again... 

... and after a half-hour of 'this... no, *that*... well, what about?...' 
he said 'Gentlemen, this is going nowhere AND THIS IS ONLY THE SCREEN 
HEADER, three or four lines of what should be systemwide standard code.  
How about we just use the same stuff that everyone sees everywhere else 
(yes, Sam, I know that Accounting will not be mentioned specifically... 
but neither will Supply so that's kind of fair) and move on to the order 
that we'll list the menu options?'

>If there are really divergent 
>requirements you provide two different interfaces.
>
>IT is a service; it is there to HELP people, not to impose solutions that 
>are technically convenient on them.

Life is a matter of Balance, Mr Dashwood... two different interfaces 
require more time and resources to develop, never mind maintain.  The 
project starts to go over deadline and over budget and since IT is the one 
who 'touches it last'... guess who gets raked over the coals?

>You may use prototyping to help them 
>visualise the end product, but you don't build ANYTHING until everyone is 
>satisfied.

Mr Dashwood, as the situation described above - a simple one, just a menu 
screen - a basic interface can, given the dynamics found in many places I 
have seen, take weeks to put together... and *still* everyone is not 
satisfied, there is always an 'Oh, I forgot about' and a 'While you're at 
it can you sort on barometric pressure?' and 'Jane's getting reports with 
three breaks, we want those, too!'.

This takes time.  This takes resources.  This takes money and *that* is 
what the organisation is in business to do... as my Sainted Paternal 
Grandfather - may he sleep with the angels! - used to say, '*Nobody* is in 
business for your good health, even the pharmacist has to make a profit'.

[snip]

>If you were a bit less cynical and a bit more accommodating you might find 
>users easier to deal with.

On the other hand... 'He who tries to satisfy everyone winds up satisfying 
no-one', hence... the definition of a well-functioning system is 'One 
which causes the person who signs my timesheets to smile'.  You may be a 
Project Leader, sure... but I have seen organisations where someone from 
Marketing goes to your Senior Gruppenoberfuehrer and says 'I've got an 
idea for Just One Little Thing and I'm getting some resistance from the 
Project Leader on it, can you do something about it?'... and then you get 
called into a Corner Office and a door closes and a Friendly Discussion 
ensues.

>
>Some of us see giving people what they want and need as a satisfying career. 

The most wonderful part of my job is when someone says to me 'You know 
that thing you did?  It makes my life So Much Easier... thanks much!'

(well... the paycheck helps, too)

>Helping them to determine those requirements is part of it, but should never 
>be allowed to drive it. When that happens you simply have IT imposing their 
>own hidden agendas on the business, as if the business were "the enemy".... 

IT is, I would say, no different than any other department.  IT exists in 
order to provide a function that helps the organisation Make Money.  IT 
does *not* exist in order to be pointed at and told 'They can't tell you 
exactly what they want so... do *something* and get them off my back'.  IT 
*is* different from many other departments in one aspect: everything must 
be reduced to ones and zeroes.  I have never heard an accoutant or a 
machine-operator or a supply-clerk say they repeatedly hear 'I know that's 
what I *told* you but it's not what I *want*'... this is a commonplace 
joke in IT.

>When the requirements are difficult that is a chance to exercise our skill 
>and imagination and achieve job satisfaction because we did something 
>extraordinary. Balancing all of the varying requirements into a coherent 
>technical architecture is why they need us. The tail does NOT wag the dog...

The dog should not be annoyed when it cannot twist the tail into a Moebius 
strip... after all, all it has to do is...

I may have mentioned this before but it might bear repeating:  About a 
quarter-century ago the General Motors Corporation (GM) subcontracted so 
much of its data processing to Electronic Data Systems (EDS) that they 
decided to buy out EDS and make it a subsidiary... this was in 1984.  One 
of the conditions of purchase imposed by the owner of EDS, one H Ross 
Perot, was that he be given a seat on GM's Board of Directors.

He was... and he began to make noise about internal conflicts between the 
various GM departments which prevented GM from doing what he believed it 
could do, 'make the best cars in the world'.  Perot, remember, came from 
an IT background.

GM was so appreciative and receptive to the suggestions of Mr Perot that 
they gave him an additional US$250M in cash and US$750M in stock to leave 
the board... and accept the condition that he would no longer talk in 
public about GM and its practises, as he did in an article in Forbes 
Magazine I mentioned here recently.  He said things like 'Starting today, 
the historic power struggle between the financial staff and the car 
builders will not be tolerated' and 'Starting today, GM's relationship 
with the UAW will be a team relationship, not an adversarial one' and 
'Starting todaym every person on the GM team will be dealt with as an 
individual, not a commodity'.

A member of the Board of Directors makes such suggestions... and they gave 
him the equivalent of US$1,000,000,000 to go away and shut up.  
Tewnty-five years later the company directors are going to the Congress 
and asking for money because their companies are no longer competitive... 
oh, and flying in their corporate jets in order to do so.

This is but one company, Mr Dashwood... and while an extreme example, 
perhaps, there are, in my experience, many, many others which exhibit 
similar traits.

DD

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: NEWS for Fujitsu COBOL users

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-12-30T14:03:17+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6rtaanF39c2hU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<xvmTk.163083$Fs5.45452@fe10.news.easynews.com> <6ofqvfF39gbeU1@mid.individual.net> <e9fb9226-6767-4376-a5bb-e557bdf62c2c@x8g2000yqk.googlegroups.com> <6rqbg7F2s2ddU1@mid.individual.net> <gjamib$2p5$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <6rqbg7F2s2ddU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> be a bit different.

I read it all.

I don't think it is harsh, just sad.

The picture is a bleak one. But I know it doesn't HAVE to be like that. 
Hence a degree of sadness.
>
>>> User interfaces are never correct because no 2 users can agree on
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> dozens of shops.

Having had decades of experience in dozens of shops, I am qualified to 
express my opinion.

> Mr Dashwood, just as you've constantly reminded me
> that 'things might, possibly, be different in other places than in
> the ones in which you've done work' so, too, might be the case for
> you.

Yes, that is possible. Nevertheless, the attitude exhibited is a completely 
negative one and is likely to encounter exactly what is being complained 
about. If you START by believing people cannot agree, allow them to see each 
other as adversaries, and approach them as being problematic, then it is 
hardly surprising that you get what you expect.

I have encountered disagreement about interfaces; it is a normal and natural 
reaction because most of us just want what we want. However, if the approach 
is that we are going to RESOLVE the differences we will inevitably 
encounter, and reach something that works for all concerned, then the way 
people approach things is ... different. The complexities of group dynamics, 
interpersonal enmity, hidden agendas etc are all part of the mix, but can be 
dealt with by someone who is astute enough to recognise them. I have NEVER 
had unresolved issues like the ones you describe, and I have NEVER allowed 
people to argue for a percentage of the time available about a triviality. I 
can immediately think of at least three strategies that would resolve the 
situation you described. The sad thing is that, apparently, no one in the 
room was capable of recognising them. (Maybe that's why I get well paid for 
what I do.)
>
>> If a system is being used by several
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> let us reason together'.

No it isn't. It is a job for EVERYONE who works in the company and receives 
a corporate paycheck. The Management part is to guide it and to approve 
choices and options which arise out of it.

Guys who "sling code" are as much a fundamental part of this process as 
department managers because the decisions reached affect EVERYBODY. It is 
when people start feeling isolated and separate and defending their own 
"domains" that the "Jobsworth" attitude you apparently advocate above 
germinates into disruptive and destructive behaviour.

"That's not my job..."

"That's a job for Management..." (Like, you're not allowed to have an 
opinion, or express it, or possibly provide more information to a process 
that you may not be directly part of, but which may affect your daily 
living?)

"That's the janitor's job..."  (Let the spilled coffee remain on the floor 
until someone slips in it and breaks a leg...)

"It's more than my job's worth to presume on someone else's turf" (Even 
though I know full well that what they are doing is stupid and 
counter-productive...)

Jobsworth. No responsibility for anything other than making sure your ass is 
covered.

I remember being a junior programmer (it was a time of sailing ships and 
steam trains... I think fire was still a novelty...) and expressing my 
opinion about a policy that a number of us thought was stupid, quietly, to 
my manager. I pointed out some of the technical problems it would involve. 
Some of the senior programmers had already recognised it but did not express 
opposition because they felt no responsibility for it, exactly as you 
described. (I was young and did not have the responsibility of a mortgage 
and putting kids through school. Like most young people I was passionate 
about stamping on stupidity... still am, but with experience I recognise 
more subtle ways to do the stamping...)

Our manager was wise. He called a meeting and got all the "code jockeys" who 
only "sling code" to describe their reservations. He then explained the 
factors which had led to the decision being taken and asked all of us to see 
if we could come up with a better solution that would take into account 
those factors we had not been aware of. We did, and the policy was modified 
by management.

What I learned from this and have never forgotten, is that decisions depend 
on information, and the best way to ensure as much information as possible 
is available, is to provide a safe environment where people are free (and 
encouraged) to contribute, irrespective of what they actually "do" in the 
company, and that every person who takes the corporate paycheck has a 
responsibility to ensure those paychecks keep coming.

Now, you might ask what would have happened if the manager I mentioned had 
NOT been wise, and had simply told me to mind my own business and get on 
with it.

We probably wouldn't be having this conversation. (I can speculate about 
what happens when someone young, articulate, and energetic, who also happens 
to be me, is told to shut up, but it is merely speculation...)

Since then I have worked in a number of places as an agent of change. I have 
seen the crumbling of outdated Victorian hierarchic management structures 
and their replacement by modern, shared responsibility, networked management 
systems. It is painful for short-sighted ignorant bullies who see 
"management" as simply a route to exercise power, but then, people like that 
deserve pain...

Certainly, not ALL organisations have raced to demolish the hierarchic 
structure, and it is very possible that the "sick shops" you describe are 
some of those.

The remnants of "military discipline" (which is actually no bad thing... it 
just gets confused with hierarchic command and control; then it becomes a 
bad thing...) still permeate many organisations and there are still many 
places where managers are not trained in management, but are promoted 
because the company needs to retain their corporate knowledge. Most of these 
people do the best they can, and some of them turn out to be excellent, but 
another percentage turn out to be abysmal. They are the ones people tend to 
remember...

It works much better when people are part of a team and feel that their 
opinions matter. Sure, a steering committee can decide the choices, but they 
will have the best possible information to make those choices on.

The same applies at the microcosm of deciding user interfaces for a number 
of departments.

I mentioned Joint Development but it wasn't recognised in your response, 
probably beause you haven't worked in places where it is practised. Many of 
the problems this thread is currently discussing are obviated by user 
involvement throughout the development process. Instead of US and THEM, 
there is only US, with every individual bringing something to the end 
product.




>
> I was told a tale, decades back, of an interface that was being
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> seniormost of the parties concerned in order to smooth things out.

Perhaps if that had been done at the start, there would have been no 
exchange of hostile memos and entrenchment into untenable positions...?

>
> In a large conference-room he set up a terminal and a projector
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> generate code.  Easy as pie, right?

Right. It is also the most frequent  misuse of RAD tools, where the desire 
to use the new "whiz bang" software, overrides the modelling and 
walkthrough. Just get stuck in and build it, our new tools can rebuild it 
instantly if we don't like it.

I am all in favour of prototyping through Joint Application Development 
workshops (which is very much what this sounds like...advanced for the 
time.), but the fatal error is to take the next step and generate a solution 
then and there before proper thought has been given to it.

What I would see coming out of this session would be recognition of a number 
of data elements which were essential to the process for all departments, 
and specific elements which would be specific to certain stakeholders. 
Interfaces to accommodate these would be sketched out by prototyping and it 
would be recognised that these were prototypes, which might be changed on 
the next iteration of the process. Outside the meeting IT would prepare 
object models by grouping the elements. This spins off into RDB design, but 
all of it is preliminary...

The next session would look at use cases and run common scenarios against 
the new objects (on a whiteboard or using suitable software) so that the 
behaviours required for the objects can be decided. At this stage we are 
looking for building blocks, not for a solution. Analysts on the team would 
liaise with each of the departments to identify all the use cases the system 
would be expected to handle,for each department, and in the next JAD 
workshop, these scenarios would be walked past the developing object models.

You can see that in such an environment, arguing about the wording of a 
header is so pathetic and trivial that no-one would bother. Besides, the 
header would be "soft" and each department could have whatever they wanted. 
User staff would work with programmers for the final iterations of the User 
interface. This necessarily coincides with the final iterations of the paper 
model.

Obviously, what I have described here is a shallow outline of MY methodology 
(which, in turn, is derived from elements of Agile, the Waterfall, and RAD, 
which I have found to be useful...) but, as I was recently accused of 
"hacking" a solution, I've taken this opportunity to show that such is not 
the case. The book I finished earlier this year covers it in depth, but I 
still haven't published it. I've simply been too busy. I'll do so in 2009.
>
> Wrong.  They started with the heading, usually something like:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> format with AM/PM suffix while another wanted a 24-hour clock...

And there were no standards in place to resolve, or at least defuse, some of 
this?

>
> ... and the IT Director had the code-jockey go through these, one at a
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> fair) and move on to the order that we'll list the menu options?'

He didn't get where he was by being stupid...

Nowadays, it is quite feasible for each department to see the menu options 
in the order that suits THEM, with the list being sorted immediately before 
it is rendered, and it is even possible for an individual user to physically 
move the entries into whatever sequence suits that person, and have this 
remembered in their individual profile. We have come a long way.
>
>> If there are really divergent
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> require more time and resources to develop, never mind maintain.

If ANY (and EVERY) interface is generated at the time it is required, with 
the sequences and fields that an individual or department has profiled, then 
the question of how many there are doesn't arise. User interface tools like 
WPF,  AJAX and Silverlight (just as examples) can do this in a few minutes 
of back-end code; if it is Web based, server side code-behind  (I use C#, 
but it isn't the only option...) can do the same job and it doesn't  take 
days to write. (OK, it took me a few days to write the COBDATA site, but I 
was out of practise and I don't claim particular expertise in ASP.NET 
anyway... :-))

The point is that two different, or any number of different, interfaces may 
not necessarily take longer to write, using modern approaches. (And even if 
they did, it would be very marginally longer, not like twice the time for 
two interfaces, etc.)


> The
> project starts to go over deadline and over budget and since IT is
> the one who 'touches it last'... guess who gets raked over the coals?
>

It doesn't on my watch, because it is timeboxed, and carefully monitored.

>> You may use prototyping to help them
>> visualise the end product, but you don't build ANYTHING until
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> 'Jane's getting reports with three breaks, we want those, too!'.

That's why User Interfaces should be flexible and NOT hard coded. If it is 
taking weeks to do this, then the tools and people need to be re-evaluated. 
Invest in people (training) and tools. If you can't or won't, then don't 
bitch about how long things take.

>
> This takes time.  This takes resources.  This takes money and *that*
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> has to make a profit'.

There is no profit in long, drawn out, IT development. The shorter and more 
flexible you can make it, the less it costs overall.

That has been the major nail in procedural development's coffin. Objects 
(components) are quicker, easier, and cheaper... even if HeyBub is not 
convinced about anythng he can't change... :-)

Eventually it comes down to trust. As you use these approaches more, you 
trust them more. If you REALLY had to change a component and didn't have the 
source for it, it is much less traumatic to write a small piece of code 
(your own replacement component) than to write half a system. This is the 
benefit of the "granularity" that components bestow. All of these things 
have a bearing on how long it takes, how much it costs to develop, and how 
much it costs to maintain...
>
> [snip]
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> satisfying no-one',

I am on record here as having stated exactly that, Mr Dwarf... :-) (On 
several occasions...)

> hence... the definition of a well-functioning
> system is 'One which causes the person who signs my timesheets to
> smile'.

No, at that point, we diverge. While I always strive to ensure that the 
person who authorises my payment (I don't usually do timesheets, because I 
don't charge by the hour - usually it is by the day, but each contract is 
negotiated, and I have worked with payment based solely on results. Although 
this is high risk, it is very lucrative if you pull it off...:-)) is happy 
with the promised results,  I do NOT connect the "definition of a 
well-functioning system" with anything other than the system functioning 
well.  It is good to have the customer smiling but that has nothing to do 
with the system functioning well... sometimes they don't actually want the 
system at all, (but must be seen to be implementing it)... corporate 
politics can be very complex.

>You may be a Project Leader, sure... but I have seen
> organisations where someone from Marketing goes to your Senior
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
LOL! I eat those occasions for breakfast... It is delightful for me to see 
the "blind sider" taken to task after the Gruppenoberfuhrer hears WHY  I am 
resisting. They never do it again. But then, I DON'T resist unless there is 
a VERY sound basis for doing so. I'd much rather say "Sure, we can do 
that..." than the alternative response, but I am careful to be certain in my 
own mind that what I am promising is achievable, before promising it.

If I have an issue with someone because I can't or won't deliver the Just 
One Little Thing, then I usually take them to lunch and lay out my case. I 
then listen to theirs and see if it can't possibly be accommodated. There 
are no entrenched positions or loss of face, just solving a problem. If we 
REALLY can't resolve it (this has happened on 2 occasions I can recall in 
nearly 20 years, and one of those was more about National Culture (Spain) 
and manners, and my failure to realise how VERY important this is to 
Spaniards), I have suggested that, as we are at an impasse, the only thing 
to do is to raise our issue to the next level of management, make our cases 
and accept the decision without rancour or petulance. (Management have a 
right to manage, even if we don't always agree with them.)

Perhaps I'm lucky (I like to think it is because I have a better case...) 
but on both of these occasions I "won". (I don't see it as a "victory" 
because I'm not at war with people who disagree with me.) In the Spanish 
case it was a dreadful (emotional and personal) price to get my way, and 
that price was paid by other people. We got a better system, but it hurt a 
number of people and I learned by it. Today, I would handle it differently.

>>  >> Some of us see giving people what they want and need as a satisfying
>> career.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> (well... the paycheck helps, too)

Exactly. I think this is "professionalism".

>
>> Helping them to determine those requirements is part of it, but
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Again, this is where JAD and iteration are valuable. They start off telling 
you what they think they want, but as it evolves they realise what is 
possible and how much better it could be. A dynamic and flexible development 
approach that can accommodate that has to be worth pursuing, in my opinion.

I think it is also true that as "computer literacy" in the general 
population has risen, people have a better idea of what can be accomplished 
than they did in the days when computers were a mystery, and all of us IT 
people were part of an arcane Priesthood that cavorted in front of the 
flashing lights, to weave spells and get results.

>> When the requirements are difficult that is a chance to exercise our
>> skill and imagination and achieve job satisfaction because we did
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It is unreasonabe for anyone to expect the dog to twist its tail into a 
Moebius strip. (especially if the problem is fleas on dog's back...). Like 
all unreasonable demands, these can be pointed out to be unreasonable and a 
more reasonable solution can be sought. For the dog to be intimidated into 
acceding to unreasonability, can only have bad results for the dog, the tail 
and the dog's owner.

> I may have mentioned this before but it might bear repeating:  About a
> quarter-century ago the General Motors Corporation (GM) subcontracted
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> commodity'.

Brave words. Probably worth the money... :-)

>
> A member of the Board of Directors makes such suggestions... and they
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> so.

I understand your frustration. I didn't realise it was that bad. The system 
is obviously flawed. The shareholders should hold the Board accountable and 
these Directors should have their personal assets sequestered to help make 
up the deficit.

We have a similar problem here where a number of investment companies have 
gone broke, destroying peoples' lives and wiping out their savings. Yet the 
Directors are still flaunting their personal wealth. As a result of the 
public outcry (and probably some non-publicised threats against property; 
you can't hide in a small community that is island based, and you can't put 
your properties on a container ship or private jet ...), and Government 
refusal to get involved (although this may change in some particular 
circumstances where it can be shown there was not negligence on the part of 
the Companies), some of these Directors (personal assets in the hundreds of 
millions, with debts in the tens of millions) are now saying they are going 
to work out rescue packages so that losses can be ameliorated. One of them 
recently made a show of selling off some expensive property assets and 
putting the proceeds into the pot...

In the GM case, the shareholders are between a rock and a hard place. They 
SHOULD insist on NO governement bailout (as taxpayers they are only paying 
for the mis-management out of taxes) but if they do, they will take a bath 
on their shareholding.

Maybe the days when angry mobs used to lynch carpet-baggers and shysters 
should be reconsidered... :-)

>
> This is but one company, Mr Dashwood... and while an extreme example,
> perhaps, there are, in my experience, many, many others which exhibit
> similar traits.
>

Sadly, I have to agree. Perhaps, as shareholders, we get what we allow and 
deserve...

Pete.

that others won't accept the status quo or think that the situations in 
their work places are inevitable and immutable. Things can change, but it 
takes people to do it, and that won't happen as long as people feel no 
reponsibility for anything other than the job they are paid to do.

Throughout history we have seen tyrants and mis-rulers removed by ordinary 
people. The same can happen in the work place. Staff do NOT have to suffer 
bad management. The COI is NOT the end of the line.

I offer opinons intended to stimulate thought; you dismiss them on the basis 
of what a marine drill sergeant told you many years ago.

Without in any way impugning the United States Marine Corps, could I gently 
suggest there may be more in Heaven and Earth than your sergeant was aware 
of? Basing your corporate life on this particular model, as an Authority, 
may not be a wise thing to do.

There isn't much we can really discuss.

Our experiences are different and so are our attitudes. I don't believe 
either of us are going to change from exchanges here. (I'm also not sure I 
would want you any different from the way you are, Mr. Dwarf... :-))

A diverse world can accommodate both of us.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-81fb3c3a00"></a>
## Thread: Access to PL22.4 documents — 1 msgs

### Access to PL22.4 documents

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-29T21:44:55-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<VOg6l.10437$tg5.910@en-nntp-05.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
to: comp.lang.cobol and OpenCOBOL people

In the past (and as of today), all people (users, vendors, whatever) who were 
interested in seeing the DETAILS of the work being done by PL22.4 (formerly J4) 
could do so by going to the web-page:

  http://www.cobolstandard.info/j4/index.htm

This provided access to such things as "drafts" of the ongoing revision work and 
details on the specific revision and defect processing that J4 / PL22.4 was (is) 
doing.

Due to either a "change in policy" or a "stricter enforcement of what they 
thought was already the policy", INCITS (the parent of PL22 which is the 
Technical Committee that is the parent of PL22.4) is now trying to "strengthen" 
the benefits of being a member and/or to limit access to "internal" documents by 
"outsiders".

To read the exact rules (as of today)  of this parent body, go to the (public) 
document at:
  http://www.incits.org/rd1/INCITS_RD1.pdf

and look for the section,
  "2.2 Policy Regarding Document Access and Membership Benefits"

The bottom-line (as I see it), is that if you now OR IN THE FUTURE, think you 
will want to see documents such as those currently posted on the PL22.4 website, 
I would suggest that you do one of the following:

A) Join PL22.4 (or get an existing member organization to designate you as an 
alternate)
  or
B) Contact the PL22.4 chair at:

    bobkarlin <at> karlinskorner.com

and request:
  - that he communicate your interest in PL22.4 going to the "public access 
model"
              and
 - that when PL22.4 does go on that model, that you would like access to the 
public documents

* * * * *

As usual, if you (like many in these groups) don't care at all about the ongoing 
COBOL Standardization process, then you need do nothing - and nothing will 
change for you!

P.S.  Rules are also changing if you think you might ever want to "visit" a 
PL22.4 meeting.  Contact me off list if you think this might impact you.

P.P.S.  WG4 (the ISO-ish group) has always had more restrictive document access 
and meeting attendance rules.  These have not changed.)
```

---

## Thread: zCobol — 2 this month / 5 total

### zCobol

- **From:** Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 2008-12-30T13:29:20-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
Those still interested in Cobol may find this link of some use:

http://www.zcobol.org/
```

#### ↳ Re: zCobol

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-12-30T18:52:36-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<qnz6l.28051$kG.14103@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<532c2624-dfc4-4c57-8ea2-9acc655da8ca@a12g2000pro.googlegroups.com>`

```
And for those interested in that page AND who are members of SHARE, you might be 
interested in this (received in the IBM Assembler group),

  ****

"Here is something new and something old for 2009. Come to SHARE session 8194 in 
Austin TX on March 3, 2009 at 8 AM for the first live demonstration of zcobol 
portable mainframe COBOL compiler which is written in z390 structured 
conditional macro assembler. zcobol supports multiple dialects of mainframe 
COBOL with the option to generate source and executable program code in any one 
of the following target languages:

1. HLASM compatible mainframe assembler for z390/z9/z10
2. Java for any J2SE platform
3. C++ for Windows platforms
4. HLA/MASM assembler for Intel platforms

The intermediate source code generated by the zcobol compiler includes all data 
labels and paragraph labels for use in debugging when required.

The initial release of zcobol is focused primarily on generating HLASM 
compatible assembler source and executable programs. The first release includes 
support for static and dynamic linking of both COBOL and assembler programs, 
EXEC CICS, and extensions for SOA messaging support to run called programs on 
remote servers. There are demo programs which can be compiled and executed in 
all 4 target language environments. And there are a rapidly growing number of 
regression tests. There is a dynamically loaded zcobol runtime module for 
complex functions and all the source code is included.

Starting March 3, 2009, zcobol will be included in z390 releases and can be 
downloaded via www.z390.org. For more information on zcobol, visit 
www.zcobol.org and join the user discussion group: 
zcobol-subscribe@yahoogroups.com
zcobol is an open source project on sourceforge.net and volunteer developers 
interested in expanding zcobol support include code generation, code 
optimization, regression testing, documentation, demos, training materials, etc. 
are all welcome. Melvyn Maltz and I will be at SHARE for the presentation and 
demo and look forward to seeing you there.

Don Higgins
don@higgins.net"
```
